Pro Private Markets
Rising Star: Tomasz Tunguz Might Be Silicon Valley's  Where's Waldo?'

By Cat Zakrzewski 
779 words
5 July 2016
15:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Regulars of San Francisco's South Park, the neighborhood where many venture-capital firms have set up West Coast outposts, often think they have spotted Redpoint Ventures Partner Tomasz Tunguz working late in the office.

But Mr. Tunguz always aims to be home for dinner with his family at 5:30 p.m. sharp. Passersby have been fooled by a cardboard cutout of him made for a technology conference that his co-workers place around the office to trick people.

It's understandable Mr. Tunguz could be perceived as being in two places at once. His experience as a Dartmouth College-educated engineer and his obsession with machine learning have led to a pace of six potentially promising investments at Redpoint.

Within Silicon Valley, the 34-year-old venture capitalist also has built a following as a writer. He has a popular VC blog and recently released his book, Winning With Data.

Tomasz Tunguz

Education

Mr. Tunguz earned a bachelor's degree in mechanical engineering, a bachelor's of engineering in machine learning and a master's in engineering management from Dartmouth College.

Career Path

After college, Mr. Tunguz worked as an engineer at Appian Corp., developing systems for the Department of Homeland Security. He then moved to Google Inc., where he managed the company's AdSense product for social networks. He joined Redpoint Ventures in 2008.

Board Positions

Mr. Tunguz sits on the boards of Axial Networks Inc., Electric Imp Inc., Expensify Inc., Looker Data Sciences Inc. and Dremio Corp. He's also a board observer at Quantifind Inc. and ThredUP Inc.

Every weekday at 5 a.m., Mr. Tunguz writes a data-driven blog post about startups. Many of his posts focus on cloud-based software companies or play off recent news, like Microsoft Corp.'s acquisition of LinkedIn Corp. Popular blog posts include "A Framework for Maximizing Startup Marketing Effectiveness" and a series called "Startup Best Practices."

Mr. Tunguz said the idea to start a blog came from attending board meetings. Entrepreneurs would ask questions about hiring or about what benchmarks they needed to hit before raising another funding round. He felt he needed to make that information available to more startups.

"As investors, we see all that data every day," Mr. Tunguz said. "We just haven't as an industry done a great job of aggregating it, collecting it and presenting it to entrepreneurs."

Mr. Tunguz said the blog has helped him build in-bound interest from entrepreneurs and given him an edge on competitive deals. Redpoint Partner Satish Dharmaraj said Mr. Tunguz has built such a strong reputation among founders that Mr. Dharmaraj often finds entrepreneurs want him on their board, even if Mr. Tunguz didn't bring the deal to Redpoint himself.

Mr. Dharmaraj has been a mentor to Mr. Tunguz and first called his idea for a blog "a yawn." He's been impressed, however, with how Mr. Tunguz has differentiated it from all the other VC blogs.

"He sets his mind to something and delivers with a ridiculous amount of hard work," Mr. Dharmaraj said.

Mr. Tunguz joined Redpoint eight years ago as an associate and rose through the ranks to general partner. He came to the firm after working as a product manager at Google Inc., overseeing AdSense for social networks.

Mr. Tunguz's bets include Looker Data Sciences Inc., Axial Networks Inc., Expensify Inc., Electric Imp Inc., ThredUP Inc. and Dremio Corp. Though they include both enterprise and consumer companies, Mr. Tunguz said the common thread among the companies is data. Looking ahead, he's interested in investing in other applications of artificial intelligence, like language processing.

Though he's proven he can edge out the competition on an investment, it remains to be seen if his unique data-driven approach will generate large returns for Redpoint. None of the companies he's invested in have exited yet.

Despite lacking a record of successful exits, he's proven he can still win competitive deals.

Looker Chief Executive Frank Bien said when his company was raising its Series A in 2013, the market was "hot" for business intelligence companies like his. He said many large venture-capital firms were interested in leading the round, but the company went with Redpoint because as a data company, they wanted to work with an investor who was so data driven.

"Venture capitalists always want to help you, Tom actually can help you," Mr. Bien said. "Tom is a long-view, build-a-business kind of a guy that's who you want around a table with you."

Pro Private Markets
Venture Capitalists Look to Corporate Acquirers, Private-Equity Firms for Exits

By Russ Garland 
1,056 words
5 July 2016
15:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Microsoft Corp.'s acquisition of LinkedIn Corp. ramped up venture-capitalist expectations that mergers and acquisitions will provide more exits for technology companies in the face of a lethargic market for initial public offerings.

Although LinkedIn was publicly traded, the $26.2 billion deal, the largest ever by Microsoft, reinforced signals that potential buyers of tech companies are on the hunt. The recent decline in the valuations of venture-backed companies could make them more attractive to corporate acquirers and private-equity firms.

"For the last three or four years, a lot of the public companies just kind of sat back and watched all the drama play out in the Valley," venture capitalist Marc Andreessen said during a recent Bloomberg LP technology conference. "Most of the big tech companies have done very well over the past five years. They've piled up lots of cash, and they have to go shopping."

The pace of venture-backed M&A activity in the second quarter approached that of last year. There were 98 transactions that closed in the quarter as of June 16, and the year-to-date total was 237 deals, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co. There were 527 M&A deals in 2015.

The disclosed value of deals is on track to beat 2015, with $20.9 billion raised through June 16, compared with $30.14 billion for all of last year. This year's pace so far is well shy of 2014, however, when disclosed deals totaled $63.52 billion.

There are plenty of targets for potential buyers. Median time from first venture investment to M&A was 4.6 years in 2015, according to VentureSource. More than 3,500 companies that received their initial venture capital from 2010 to 2012 are private and independent, according to data compiled by VentureSource in May.

Private-company pricing remains a concern for buyers, however. Although valuations have come down from last year's peak, many investors think they will fall farther. "I think it's going to take a year to get all these valuations adjusted, if not two," said Hans Swildens, founder and chief executive of secondary investor Industry Ventures LLC.

LinkedIn went public in 2011 after raising more than $100 million in venture capital. Its stock peaked at $270.76 a share in February 2015 but one year later dropped as low as $100.98 each.

Microsoft agreed to pay $196 a share in a cash deal that was seen as an effort by the software company to bolster its Office products by linking them to a professional social network that claims 433 million members.

Microsoft in March acquired Xamarin Inc., a five-year-old maker of software development tools. The price wasn't disclosed but a person familiar with the deal told The Wall Street Journal that the company went for between $400 million and $500 million. Venture investors included CRV, Floodgate, Ignition Partners and Insight Venture Partners.

Other active corporate acquirers include Cisco Systems Inc., which in March paid $1.4 billion for Jasper Technologies Inc. The company's technology monitors sensors via cellular networks, enabling customers to track assets such as vehicles and industrial equipment, an application of the so-called Internet of Things. Jasper received its first venture capital in 2005 and counted Sequoia Capital among its investors.

Apple Inc. acquired three venture-backed companies so far this year: Emotient Inc., Flyby Media Inc. and LearnSprout Inc. Emotient uses artificial-intelligence technology to analyze facial expressions. It raised $8 million from venture investors including Intel Capital, but the company hadn't been able to secure additional financing at favorable terms, according to someone familiar with the matter.

Private-equity firms also are acquiring venture-backed companies. One notable deal was Vista Equity Partners' purchase of Ping Identity Corp. for an undisclosed amount. The deal is expected to close in the third quarter.

The internet-security company, launched in 2002, was preparing to go public in 2017 when Vista showed up, according to Chief Executive Andre Durand. He said Ping had been approached by potential buyers about a half dozen times and accepted Vista's offer because it enabled Ping to remain a standalone company. Its venture backers included Draper Fisher Jurvetson and General Catalyst Partners.

Around the same time as the Ping announcement, Vista agreed to acquire publicly traded Marketo Inc., a marketing technology provider, for about $1.79 billion. Like LinkedIn, Marketo was a former venture-backed company whose stock price had fallen early this year.

Vista also this year bought venture-backed company Telarix Inc. and late last year purchased Fiverun Inc. Prices weren't disclosed.

The firm merged point-of-sale software company Fiverun with two other portfolio companies to form Kibo Software Inc., a provider of online-commerce software. Fiverun had raised a small first round in 2014 from seed investor Bee Partners and accelerator Orange Fab.

Telarix, which makes software used by communications services providers, raised $20 million in growth equity in 2010 from Brown Brothers Harriman Capital Partners and Edison Partners. Edison initially backed the company in 2003.

Vista is reportedly seeking up to $10 billion for its latest buyout fund to invest in technology companies.

Another private-equity firm with deep pockets is Thoma Bravo LLC, which is said to be loading up its latest fund with up to $7.2 billion. In June, Thoma Bravo said it agreed to acquire Qlik Technologies Inc. for about $3 billion. The data-analytics software company went public in 2010. One of the firm's other portfolio companies, Landesk Software Inc., has made seven acquisitions in the past five years. The most recent was AppSense Inc., a former venture-backed company that helped businesses manage desktop software.

Thoma Bravo in September took a majority stake in MedeAnalytics Inc., a provider of software used to analyze health-care financial data, for an undisclosed amount. Venture firms Bain Capital Ventures and Emergence Capital stayed on as minority investors.

Buyouts of venture-backed companies, relatively rare until after the dot-com bubble, have become more frequent. There were 53 in 2014, 50 in 2015 and 31 so far this year, according to VentureSource.

Markets
Social-Media ETFs Try to Tweet the Market; These funds use tweets and likes to decide where to invest

By Gerrard Cowan 
962 words
5 July 2016
03:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Social media is a gigantic store of information. But can tweets, likes and posts be used to pick stocks?

Although some analysts point to the need for caution, a number of funds have sprung up that seek to use social-media information to generate returns. Perhaps the clearest example of this trend is Sprott Buzz Social Media Insights ETF (ticker symbol BUZ), which was launched in April. BUZ tracks the Buzz Social Media Insights Index, which analyzes data from social-media comments, blog posts and news articles to identify 25 stocks about which the online community is most bullish.

The Buzz index uses an analytics model that deploys artificial intelligence and algorithms intended to understand the tone of a conversation even including sarcasm. The index is reset every month, and a company must have a market cap of at least $5 billion to be included, though the stocks being talked about online tend to belong to the largest companies anyway, says Jamie Wise, the founder of Buzz Indexes.

The group knows best

BUZ's managers won't intervene, even if a stock appears in the index that they don't personally rate.

"That collective group decision-making is better than any one individual's ability to pick a stock, and so we're not going to insert ourselves on top of that process," says Mr. Wise.

The use of social-media data to inform investments is part of a broader trend toward exploiting big data, or very large sets of information that can be used to identify patterns or trends.

BlackRock Inc.'s Scientific Active Equity team uses big-data analysis in a number of its funds, says Jeff Shen, co-head of investments in the SAE Group and the head of emerging markets at the company. For example, individual investors dominate the Chinese stock market, and it can be difficult to figure out what they are thinking about, he says. The SAE group gathers information from the most popular social-media sites in the country in a bid to gain insight into a market that has been historically difficult to gauge.

"When there are a lot of people talking about one particular stock, it will probably give you a sense of the retail attention in that particular name or particular sector," says Mr. Shen.

Vote bullish or bearish

Other funds seek to gather data directly. CrowdInvest Wisdom ETF (WIZE) tracks an index of 35 stocks that are selected every month based on data gathered from an iOS app. It can be thought of as Tinder (a location-based dating app) for investing, says CrowdInvest Chief Executive Annie Wyatt: Users rate stocks as bullish or bearish, and the most-favored stocks are placed into the index.

The app is based on "Wisdom of the Crowd" theory. Ms. Wyatt and her colleagues place no restrictions on who can download the app and vote, meaning a newcomer will be given the same power as an experienced long-term investor. The idea is that the group beats the wisdom of an individual.

"We want to make sure that people from all walks of life are coming into the app and voting and expressing their opinions, and we don't ask for any reason why they're expressing these opinions," she says.

The potential of all that data is one of the attractions for investors when it comes to social-media companies themselves, says Jay Jacobs, director of research at Global X, whose Social Media Index ETF (SOCL) tracks the performance of the Solactive Social Media Total Return Index.

"There are a lot of different uses for that data, and it is a big component of revenues for these companies, so it should not be overlooked as an area of growth," he says.

PureFunds ISE Big Data ETF (BIGD) aims to track the companies that are mining and analyzing the data. The fund was launched in July 2015, with the aim of tapping into the growing industry, say PureFunds CEO Andrew Chanin.

"I think people are going to become aware of how big this industry is. & If you're watching the financial news, it is tough to go an hour without hearing companies promoting their big-data solutions or analysts talking about the space or companies in the space," he says.

Critics weigh in

Still, the use of social-media data and big data more broadly has its critics. While social media can be an important source of information and can capture trends in the market, it will prompt most serious investors to carry out their own research, rather than relying on it directly for making an investment decision, says Neena Mishra, director of ETF Research at Zacks Investment Research.

"I don't think these can supplant the human analyst, at least not in the near term," she says. "True, machines can process and analyze data much quicker compared with humans, but the challenge lies in deciding the importance of each piece of information in the investment decision."

Kiril Nikolaev, a financial analyst at "http://etfdb.com/", also believes that while big-data analysis is increasingly important, it has potential drawbacks. For example, if everyone starts using the same or similar models to analyze information, the advantage will soon be "crowded out," he says.

Mr. Cowan is a writer in London. He can be reached at"mailto:reports@wsj.com".

Uber Rival Grab Gains Ground in Southeast Asia; Singapore-based GrabTaxi operates in more Southeast Asian cities than Uber, and is winning the race for riders

By Newley Purnell 
1,038 words
5 July 2016
13:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Uber Technologies Inc. is locked in major tussles with local rivals in China and India, but a homegrown upstart is also grabbing an advantage in the race for another Asia prize.

A startup called Grab is winning ride-hailing turf in Southeast Asia home to 600 million people, almost double the population of the U.S. The startup serves more cities in the region than Uber and, according to mobile-app analytics firm App Annie, is beating the world's most valuable startup in the race for users here.

The region's ride-hailing market is forecast to grow more than five times to $13.1 billion by 2025 from $2.5 billion last year, according to a recent report on Southeast Asia's internet economy conducted by Alphabet Inc.'s Google and Singapore state-investment firm Temasek Holdings.

Singapore-based GrabTaxi Holdings Pte Ltd., as it is formally known, launched in 2012 and offers locally tailored services such as motorcycle taxis a popular method for negotiating the region's traffic-clogged cities package deliveries and cash payment. It also has a pilot carpooling service allowing people living in southern Malaysia, where living costs are lower than in neighboring Singapore, to share rides into the city-state.

In four years, the company, valued at $1.6 billion in its most recent funding round last year, has expanded to 1,600 employees. It operates in 30 Southeast Asian cities twice as many as Uber in six countries.

Even so, Grab faces a tough fight against Uber, a global powerhouse valued at close to $68 billion. It has raised $14 billion in debt and equity, providing it with a massive war chest for global expansion and local turf wars.

A spokesman said Uber is "part of everyday life for millions across Southeast Asia," where the company is "led by local teams."

Grab has some significant investors in its corner. In addition to U.S. hedge fund Coatue Management LLC and Tiger Global Management, it has received funding from Uber's biggest competitor in China: Didi Chuxing Technology Co., the country's largest ride-sharing company.

Grab has also joined Didi, ANI Technologies Pvt.'s Ola in India and San Francisco startup Lyft Inc. in a global alliance of Uber rivals. They work together to allow users of each company's app to hail rides from the other apps' drivers in other countries.

Grab's co-founder, Anthony Tan, a 34-year-old Malaysian who dreamed up the venture with a classmate at Harvard Business School, said he envisions the company as a sprawling, web-based platform, leveraging its location data to offer services beyond rides, such as digital payments.

"We don't think only about transport," said Mr. Tan, also Grab's chief executive, in an interview at the company's Singapore headquarters, a modest space in an office park where new taxi drivers come to sign up. "How do we build a Baidu, Alibaba, Tencent?" he said, referring to China's three leading internet companies, which dominate that country's search, e-commerce and social-media markets.

Grab has banked close to two-thirds of the almost $700 million it has raised from investors and isn't in need of additional funds at the moment, Mr. Tan said. The company is profitable in some cities in which it operates, he said, though he declined to forecast when the company will turn a profit overall.

In a region where few people have credit cards and many lack bank accounts, Grab first offered cash payments for users and drivers alike. It recently added motorbike taxis in cities like Ho Chi Minh City and Jakarta so commuters use can weave through traffic gridlock. Uber has since followed.

Grab also offers a package-delivery service designed to assist the region's e-commerce startups, which sometimes struggle to get items to customers in places that lack traditional addresses or where roads are poor. Uber has a delivery service, but it isn't available outside the U.S.

In Indonesia, Grab has worked to augment third-party maps by adding points of interest for pickups and drop-offs at locations not otherwise covered, like warungs roadside stalls where people often congregate to buy goods or socialize.

Uber's innovations include the UberEats on-demand food-delivery service, which gained its first Asian location in May with a launch in Singapore. The company's carpool service, UberPool, was expanded from Jakarta and Manila to Singapore last week.

Uber's huge cash pile means it can afford to hire top engineering talent and develop cutting-edge technologies. It could also allow it to spend money for longer than the less-deep-pocketed Grab, analysts say, potentially wearing it down over the long haul.

Then there is a technological barrier both companies face: While Southeast Asia's population is large, many consumers still lack web access, meaning they can't use the apps.

Neither Grab nor Uber discloses user numbers, but Grab's app topped Uber's in first-quarter downloads for both Android and Apple devices in every market in which Grab operates: Indonesia, Malaysia, the Philippines, Singapore, Thailand and Vietnam, according to App Annie.

Grab also leads Uber in monthly active users in "a number of Southeast Asian markets" on Android smartphones, which dominate the region, App Annie estimates. The company doesn't give out numbers.

Grab says its app has been downloaded more than 15 million times. An Uber spokesman declined to comment on App Annie's data, or disclose any user or download data for Southeast Asia.

Florian Hoppe, a consultant at Bain & Company, said "it's hard for [Uber] to tailor to unique local requirements" in the region, while Singapore-based Grab understands what Southeast Asian consumers need. He pointed to Grab's service connecting commuters in Malaysia to Singapore as an example.

"That's the gap" Grab has taken advantage of, he said.

Yuan Yang in Beijing

3 July 2016
08:09

FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The co-founder of China s first privately owned bank is turning his attention to a new internet finance venture, saying that the traditional banking sector cannot be relied upon to support entrepreneurial businesses.

 Our banks and financial institutions serve the state-owned sector and the government,  Liu Yonghao, a self-made agribusiness tycoon and co-founder of Minsheng Bank, told the Financial Times.  From the time they were founded, they lacked the objective of providing funding for private enterprises. 

Mr Liu added:  The banking sector isn t fit for the age we live in , noting that privately owned businesses now account for 60 per cent of China s economic output.

Chinese banks have traditionally preferred to lend to large state-owned businesses, but some economists say the problem has become worse in recent years. Non-performing loans have increased over the past five years, making bankers more hesitant to lend to private sector entrepreneurs.

The problem is even more acute in rural areas, according to Mr Liu, who made his initial fortune in the agricultural sector. Mr Liu s New Hope Group was established in Sichuan province in 1982.

Farmers are not allowed to own land but instead lease it from the state, so cannot use it as collateral to secure bank loans.

 A big ship is difficult to turn round,  said Mr Liu,  [China s state] banks want to change but are too big to do so. 

Mr Liu has a "http://www.forbes.com/profile/liu-yonghao/" and is ranked number 28 in China on the Forbes Billionaires List from 2016.

The Chinese government has identified the private sector s funding challenges as a vital policy challenge. Last year it started a pilot programme that has approved the establishment of seven privately owned banks.

Mr Liu s new internet finance venture, Sichuan Hope Bank, received its final regulatory approvals in June. It is a joint venture with Xiaomi, the smartphone manufacturer, and a Sichuan-based supermarket chain, Chengdu Hongqi.

Mr Liu and his partners embraced an online business model to avoid the costs associated with bricks-and-mortar branches. Credit risk will be assessed through automated systems in an effort to hasten lending approvals and level the playing field for small start-ups.

Sichuan Hope Bank is not the first to embrace mobile banking platforms and use  big data  to assess client risks. Tencent s WeBank, for example, reviews loan applicants  comments and posts on its popular WeChat messaging service.

New Hope Group has a record of lending to rural start-ups: eight years ago, it branched out from selling pig feed and equipment to providing trade financing for farmers who wanted to set up small pig-farming factories.

Although the farmers would come to New Hope without business experience, Mr Liu felt he could identify the successful type.  They could understand markets from doing labour in the cities,  he said.

But Mr Liu said he felt confident lending to them, especially former migrant workers who had returned home.

Markets
Why Finra Pays Its Executives So Much; Unlike the SEC and other government entities, Wall Street's self-regulator aims to compensate top officials in line with what they would earn at banks and brokers

By Dave Michaels 
827 words
1 July 2016
22:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority, which "http://www.wsj.com/articles/top-wall-street-regulators-salary-approaches-3-million-1467389380", doesn't pay to compete with other rule writers it compensates its executives like Wall Street does.

Finra, which oversees stockbrokers and monitors trading on equity and options exchanges, "http://www.finra.org/sites/default/files/2015_YIR_AFR.pdf" that eight of its senior executives earned more than $1 million in total compensation last year. It benchmarks its salaries against "a broad section of financial services and capital markets companies, as this is the most likely sector from which Finra will recruit talent," according to its most recent annual report.

At the same time, the Wall Street overseer acknowledges there aren't many substitutes for what it does, meaning a securities lawyer at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. wouldn't do the same job at Finra. "Defining the relevant employment market for competitive compensation benchmarking purposes is a significant challenge for Finra due to the scarcity of natural comparisons," it said in the annual report.

CEO Richard Ketchum, who has announced he is retiring later this year, earned $1 million in base salary and $1.5 million in incentive compensation. The rest of his pay came from deferred compensation and other benefits, according to the report issued Thursday. Before joining Finra, he was CEO of the regulatory arm of the New York Stock Exchange. He also worked on Wall Street as general counsel of Citigroup Inc.'s corporate and investment bank from 2003 to 2004.

Chief Financial Officer Todd Diganci, who was paid nearly $1.4 million, has worked at Finra for 21 years, according to his LinkedIn resume. Chief Information Officer Steven Randich, who got nearly $1.3 million, was co-CIO of Citigroup before joining Finra, his LinkedIn biography shows.

The regulator's board of directors awarded those salaries even as Finra posted a net loss of $39.5 million for 2015, driven by what it said were bigger expenses for cloud-based computing investments. Compensation and benefits expenses increased 5.5% in 2015 from 2014.

In a letter issued with Finra's annual report, Mr. Ketchum wrote that his organization's responsibilities and technology prowess has grown significantly since he took over in 2009. That year, the market regulator monitored on average 2.3 billion quotes, orders and trade each day. It now processes as many as 75 billion pieces of market data every day.

"This dramatic growth in Finra's ability to process and monitor massive amounts of data is reflective of the tremendous steps we have taken over the past seven years to improve our regulatory oversight of the complex and interconnected U.S. securities markets," Mr. Ketchum wrote.

Finra occupies a unique position in the realm of market regulation. While it is not a government agency, it can penalize brokers for regulatory violations. Rules that it passes, which affect how brokers interact with their customers, must be approved by the Securities and Exchange Commission, whose chairman, Mary Jo White, earns $165,300.

At first glance, Finra's decision to compare its salaries to Wall Street's looks reasonable, said Alan Johnson, a compensation consultant for financial-services companies. But a private regulatory organization doesn't face the same checks on pay that businesses and law firms do, such as angry shareholders or clients that push back on big legal bills, he said.

"I don't think it's thought of as well as the SEC, and the SEC does not pay comparable with industry," said Mr. Johnson, managing director of Johnson Associates. "For the money they're paying, are they getting the results and accountability that some would expect? I think within the industry that is certainly an open question."

Earlier in his career, Mr. Ketchum was a top brokerage and exchange regulator at the SEC. Other former SEC officials, including Finra's Chief Risk Officer Carlo di Florio and Erozan Kurtas, hired as Finra's head of advanced data analytics, have left the government and moved directly to Finra, where salaries are much higher.

Some U.S. lawmakers have in recent months questioned Finra's duties and the SEC's ability to oversee it. In a letter issued June 6, Sen. John Boozman (R., Ark.) and Rep. Ander Crenshaw (R., Fla.) demanded Ms. White explain how the SEC oversees Finra's budget and its enforcement function.

Mr. Ketchum will be succeeded by Robert Cook, a former SEC official who is a partner at law firm Cleary Gottlieb Steen & Hamilton LLP. Mr. Cook is scheduled to become Finra's CEO in late August or early September. Finra has declined to disclose Mr. Cook's pay until next year, when it issues its next annual report.

The Rise of Stress-Test Inc.

By Ryan Tracy 
1,058 words
30 June 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B5
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- After Citigroup Inc. unexpectedly failed the Federal Reserve's annual stress tests in March 2014, the bank opened its checkbook and called in the consultants.

The firm had to address several of the regulator's concerns in a short time frame, and no single consulting firm could do the job, according to people familiar with the matter. The bank hired multiple firms and said it spent about $180 million on stress tests during the second half of 2014. The next year, it passed.

The stress tests -- designed to determine whether banks can withstand severe economic shocks -- have made U.S. banks stronger. Also more robust is the multibillion-dollar industry that has developed around the annual exercise. Last week, the Fed said the 33 largest U.S. banks cleared the first round of stress tests. On Wednesday, the firms will find out which ones can boost dividends or buy back shares.

Globally, banks spent about $29 billion on consultants last year, much of that for stress tests in the U.S. and elsewhere, according to analysis firm ALM Intelligence. That compares with $16.35 billion in 2007. The total has increased every year since 2009, a period when banks have been relentlessly cutting expenses and employees in areas such as trading and branches.

The Big Four accounting firms and Big Three consulting firms all have built practices offering manpower and advice to pass regulatory muster. Big financial advisers are also in on the act. Last year, BlackRock Inc. sold forecasting help to about two-thirds of the banks taking the tests.

There are even consultants offering advice on the most economical use of other stress-test consultants.

"Kick out the consultants," risk analysis firm Novantas advertised recently, telling banks it can help them cut back.

The industry has sprung up because failing the stress tests, which the Fed made mandatory in their current form in 2011, can have grave consequences. Banks that don't pass the test can be limited in their ability to raise dividends or buy back shares.

The early versions of the tests helped stem the global financial crisis in 2009 by showing big banks weren't about to collapse. Since then, both regulators and bankers said the exams have made banks such as Citigroup better able to weather shocks like the British vote to leave the European Union.

One executive said this year's scenario -- which included negative interest rates, along with a 10% unemployment rate and a roughly 50% drop in the stock market -- helped catch flaws in systems that weren't ready to compute rates below zero.

But many question whether the Fed is going overboard. Rob Nichols, president of industry group the American Bankers Association, last week called the tests "unrealistically severe" and "unnecessarily opaque." The Fed has made the tests harder to pass each year and surprised banks such as Citigroup by flunking them for risk-management practices. Hence, the need for heavy spending each round to adapt to the new versions of the tests.

Banks are expected to quantify precisely how much money they would lose under the scenarios, a herculean task for firms with global footprints, taking hundreds of mathematical models to predict the behavior of millions of assets. Each loan or investment has unique characteristics a bank must gather, track, check and recheck.

Banks generally underinvested in those skills before 2008, one reason they were hit so hard by the downturn.

The test submissions include a "narrative" document the size of a thick paperback, describing what the firm has done, accompanied by thousands of pages of documentation. Some banks employ professional writers to pen the narratives' chapters, so the Fed will have fewer questions when it examines the firms.

In all, building a stress-test program can cost banks $150 million to $250 million apiece at the start.

Financial firms globally will spend $4 billion on stress-test information technology this year, estimates Chartist Research. That is up from $3 billion in 2014 and is expected to increase 15% in 2017.

Forecasting models generally range in cost from about $100,000 to more than $1 million, depending on their complexity.

"We offer customized and off-the-shelf models to help you calculate the stressed probability of defaults," reads a "Stress Testing Suites" brochure from Moody's Analytics Inc., a unit of Moody's Corp.

The banks taking the Fed exams treat stress-test preparation like its own business line, often paying the executives who lead the effort seven-figure salaries.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the largest U.S. bank by assets, last year had about 550 people working solely on the Fed stress tests, with more than 2,000 employees contributing indirectly, according to a person familiar with the matter.

"It's like a whole new department at the bank," said Joe Sullivan, president of International Market Recruiters, which helps banks fill stress-testing jobs.

Stress has become a problem among stress-test employees, who often race to finish the exams before the Fed's deadline. The night before submissions are due, bank employees have had to stay up loading documents onto the Fed's slow-moving website, one of many late nights.

The test due date for years was January, notoriously ruining bankers' year-end holidays. In sympathy, the Fed this year changed the tests' due date to April, though one banker pointed out that conflicted with spring breaks instead.

Ultimately, the goal is for banks to scale back this mammoth exercise, for executives to have a kind of "push-button" ability to quickly assess their risk, said Michael Alix, who was a senior executive at Bear Stearns during its near-collapse in 2008 and a former Federal Reserve Bank of New York official who now advises banks on the tests as financial services consulting risk leader at PricewaterhouseCoopers.

Banks continue to build up their teams.

The posting for a Citigroup job in Mumbai analyzing the bank's risks there and reporting up to the U.S. parent asks for: "Strong attention to detail, willingness to 'roll up sleeves' and produce a polished, high quality, accurate product; tireless work ethic with ability to work well under pressure."

---

Emily Glazer and Sarah Krouse contributed to this article.

INSIDE BUSINESS
LSE-Deutsche Brse merger dead in the water after Brexit vote

By Sarah Gordon in Europe 
801 words
30 June 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Anyone who believes that the merger between Germany's Deutsche Brse and the London Stock Exchange is going ahead must have their head buried in the sand. Just like the people who designed the deal.

It was meant to bring together London, Europe's leading financial hub, with Frankfurt, the home of the European Central Bank, to create a behemoth able to trade in equities and futures, and supply benchmarks, index and data analytics. When it was first announced in February, both sides were publicly dismissive of the impact of a British vote to leave the EU - and it appears the deal terms were designed with similar insouciance. These terms cannot now be changed - making the likelihood of the deal closing vanishingly small, despite reassuring utterances by both companies to the contrary.

It is not unusual for the architects of a deal to become so fixated on getting it done that they fail to focus on what might derail it. Mergers and acquisitions history is littered with the corpses of overconfident companies that were unable to get their deals from the drawing board to the board room. Nor are Deutsche Brse and the LSE the only members of the business community to have underestimated and underprepared for a Brexit. A lack of contingency planning as well as a fastidious refusal to dirty their hands in the political debate have left many other businesses scrambling to react to the vote. A survey of corporate clients by one big advisory group on Friday found that a staggering four-fifths had neither a Brexit response committee set up, nor a specific plan for what to do if the outcome was for Leave.

This complacency is shocking. But more so at Deutsche Brse and the LSE. When the deal was unveiled, advisers to both were keen to suggest that their timing was cunning. This bid, they said, had been deliberately launched amid the political uncertainty in the run-up to the referendum to "steal a march on US rivals". They said the risk of Brexit "provided the camouflage" under which the deal terms were finalised. Quite how clever that was is now moot.

Since Friday, both companies have put a reassuring gloss on the referendum news. They were committed, according to statements on Friday and again yesterday, to the deal's agreed and binding terms, irrespective of the vote, and the outcome did not affect its "compelling strategic rationale". Behind the scenes, though, it was a different story, with senior managers at the LSE privately acknowledging that the deal would be the first corporate casualty of the vote.

There was good reason for their gloom: those "agreed and binding terms". Because they cannot be tinkered with, and because they state that the combined companies' headquarters will be in London, they make the deal highly unlikely to close. German regulators - not to mention politicians - were already queasy at the prospect of a London HQ, and are even more likely to put their foot down now given that, after a Brexit, London's financial activities will not be overseen by Europe. Putting the HQ of the merged company in a city no longer able to trade freely with the bloc also reduces the financial logic of the deal - as does the sharp fall in sterling since Friday.

A particular spanner in the works is likely to be the European Central Bank's demand that the future clearing of euro-denominated derivatives transactions takes place inside the EU. That would entail routing trades through a city such as Paris or Frankfurt, rather than London.

All these consequences to a Brexit vote were foreseeable at the time the deal was announced and its terms were designed. But this was a pet project of Xavier Rolet , the chief executive of the LSE and it provided a dramatic start to Carsten Kengeter 's new job as chief executive of Deutsche Brse. Such considerations, it appears, trumped any proper evaluation of the risks.

Millions of pounds will already have been spent on lawyers, PR executives and other advisers to this deal, as well as thousands of management hours. Messrs Kengeter and Rolet are unlikely to take personal responsibility for the deal falling through, rather as the UK's political leaders have brushed off their share of the blame for last week's referendum outcome. A six-person referendum committee drawn from the boards of both companies and led by Joachim Faber, Deutsche Brse's supervisory board chairman, will now meet to "work through the challenges" the Brexit vote has posed.

This will be a further waste of time and shareholders' money for a deal that no longer makes sense.

sarah.gordon@ft.com

Finra Fines Deutsche Bank $6 Million Over Trading Data; Regulator says German bank didn't offer timely and accurate  blue sheet' data as required

By Anna Prior and Brittney Laryea 
407 words
30 June 2016
13:43
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority said Wednesday it "http://www.finra.org/newsroom/2016/finra-fines-deutsche-bank-securities-inc-6-million-submitting-inaccurate-and-late-blue" AG's securities arm $6 million for failing to provide timely and accurate trade data, the largest penalty from Wall Street's self-regulator for issues related to so-called blue sheets.

Finra said it and the Securities and Exchange Commission regularly request certain trade data, also known as blue sheets, to assist in the investigation of market manipulation and insider trading, and the bank didn't offer in a timely manner its data in an automated format as required.

"There's increasing pressure on regulators to provide more real-time surveillance, and any delay in data compromises our ability to do that," said Finra Executive Vice President Cameron Funkhouser. "Consistent failure to produce accurate data is just unacceptable."

A Deutsche Bank spokeswoman declined to comment. The firm didn't admit or deny the charges, but consented to the entry of the findings, according to Finra. The bank also has agreed to employ an independent consultant to help improve its procedures related to blue-sheet submissions, said Finra.

Data analytics have become a driving force in regulation, with proponents arguing that access to timely and accurate data can allow regulators to act more swiftly to stop activity that could hurt investors.

As such, regulators have been looking closely at data production from brokerages and brought a number of enforcement cases against firms in recent years.

Late last year, Finra fined a unit of Macquarie Group Ltd. $2.95 million for inaccurate blue-sheet data, while the U.S. arm of Credit Suisse Group AG"http://www.wsj.com/articles/credit-suisse-in-sec-pact-over-blue-sheet-submissions-1443469915" in a settlement with the SEC in September.

For Deutsche Bank, Finra said it found the firm had multiple failures in the system it uses to put together and submit its blue-sheet data between 2008 and 2015, affecting thousands of submissions and more than 1 million trades.

The regulator also found that between January 2014 and August 2015 about 40% of the global investment bank's blue-sheet submissions missed the required 10-business-day deadline for responding to requests. That portion was 90% between July and August 2015.

LSE-Deutsche Brse dealmakers were wrong to ignore Brexit risk

Sarah Gordon 
801 words
29 June 2016
05:04
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Anyone who believes that the merger between Germany s Deutsche Brse and the London Stock Exchange is going ahead must have their head buried in the sand. Just like the people who designed the deal.

It was meant to bring together London, Europe s leading financial hub, with Frankfurt, the home of the European Central Bank, to create a behemoth able to trade in equities and futures, and supply benchmarks, index and data analytics. When it was first announced in February, both sides were publicly dismissive of the impact of a British vote to "http://www.ft.com/thirdpartywrapper/webappliveeurope"   and it appears the deal terms were designed with similar insouciance. These terms cannot now be changed   making the likelihood of the deal closing vanishingly small, despite reassuring utterances by both companies to the contrary.

It is not unusual for the architects of a deal to become so fixated on getting it done that they fail to focus on what might derail it. Mergers and acquisitions history is littered with the corpses of overconfident companies that were unable to get their deals from the drawing board to the board room. Nor are Deutsche Brse and the LSE the only members of the business community to have underestimated and underprepared for a Brexit. A lack of contingency planning as well as a fastidious refusal to dirty their hands in the political debate have left many other businesses scrambling to react to the vote. A survey of corporate clients by one big advisory group on Friday found that a staggering four-fifths had neither a Brexit response committee set up, nor a specific plan for what to do if the outcome was for Leave.

This complacency is shocking. But more so at Deutsche Brse and the LSE. When the deal was unveiled, advisers to both were keen to suggest that their timing was cunning. This bid, they said, had been deliberately launched amid the political uncertainty in the run-up to the referendum to  steal a march on US rivals . They said the risk of Brexit  provided the camouflage  under which the deal terms were finalised. Quite how clever that was is now moot.

Since Friday, both companies have put a reassuring gloss on the news of the referendum s result. They were committed, according to joint statements on Friday and again on Wednesday, to the deal s agreed and binding terms, irrespective of the vote, and the outcome did not affect its  compelling strategic rationale .

Behind the scenes, though, it was a different story, with senior managers at the LSE privately acknowledging that the deal would be the first corporate casualty of the vote.

There was good reason for their gloom: those  agreed and binding terms . Because they cannot be tinkered with, and because they state that the combined companies  headquarters will be in London, they make the deal highly unlikely to close. German regulators   not to mention politicians   were already queasy at the prospect of a London HQ, and are even more likely to put their foot down now given that, after a Brexit, London s financial activities will not be overseen by Europe.

Putting the HQ of the merged company in a city no longer able to trade freely with the bloc also reduces the financial logic of the deal   as does the sharp fall in sterling since Friday.

A particular spanner in the works is likely to be the European Central Bank s demand that the future clearing of euro-denominated derivatives transactions takes place inside the EU. That would entail routing trades through a city such as Paris or Frankfurt, rather than London.

All these consequences to a Brexit vote were foreseeable at the time the deal was announced and its terms were designed. But this was a pet project of Xavier Rolet, the chief executive of the LSE and it provided a dramatic start to Carsten Kengeter s new job as chief executive of Deutsche Brse. Such considerations, it appears, trumped any proper evaluation of the risks.

Millions of pounds will already have been spent on lawyers, PR executives and other advisers to this deal, as well as thousands of management hours. Messrs Kengeter and Rolet are unlikely to take personal responsibility for the deal falling through, rather as the UK s political leaders have brushed off their share of the blame for last week s referendum outcome. A six-person referendum committee drawn from the boards of both companies and led by Joachim Faber, Deutsche Brse s supervisory board chairman, will now meet to  work through the challenges  the Brexit vote has posed.

This will be a further waste of time and shareholders  money for a deal that no longer makes sense.

The Property Report
A Brexit Boost: Mortgages --- Treasury yields fall, pulling down rates, with many expecting refinancings to pick up

By Laura Kusisto 
607 words
29 June 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Britain's decision to leave the European Union could benefit a group thousands of miles away: U.S. homeowners.

Several lenders posted rates for 30-year, fixed-rate mortgages of about 3.5% on Monday, nearing a 3.5-year low, and analysts expect coming reports to show that average U.S. mortgage rates have decreased since the Brexit vote Thursday.

The main reason: Investors have flocked to the safety of U.S. Treasurys, pushing interest rates lower as riskier assets such as stocks tumbled. Mortgage rates tend to move up and down with 10-year Treasury rates, though the relationship isn't perfect.

The yield on the 10-year Treasury note was 1.460% on Tuesday, down from 1.579% before the result of Thursday's vote was announced and 2.273% at the end of 2015, according to Tradeweb.

Last Wednesday, the Mortgage Bankers Association reported that the average rate for a 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage was 3.76%. Housing-market analysts will be watching closely on Wednesday when the MBA releases its next weekly report.

If mortgage rates decline, that could be a boon both to prospective home buyers and to homeowners who qualify for refinancing. Refinancing could make sense for many people locked into rates of 4% or higher, according to mortgage experts.

Lenders are expecting a miniwave of refinancing in coming weeks. In all, 40% of borrowers have loans with a rate of 4.5% or higher, according to CoreLogic Inc., a real-estate analytics firm, meaning they could save about $90 a month on average by refinancing at 3.5%.

Lenders across the country said refinancing applications since Thursday are up between 10% to 40% compared with typical volume this time of year.

"Unless we see interest rates tick back up, I think we will see a boomlet" in refinancing activity, said Bob Walters, chief economist at Detroit-based Quicken Loans Inc., the country's third-largest mortgage lender. Mr. Walters said Quicken is seeing refinancing applications up 30% to 40% compared with typical volume.

Because mortgage rates have been near multidecade lows for the past six years, most borrowers who could refinance already have done so. Those who held back largely did so because their home was still underwater or their credit was poor.

But strong home-price growth in the past several months has helped increase the pool of borrowers with equity in their homes who are eligible to refinance. About 13% of homeowners owe more than their home is worth, down from a high of 31% in 2012, according to Zillow, a real-estate research firm.

The Brexit vote is expected to usher in a period of economic uncertainty across Europe as companies and investors pause to weigh the impact and seek out safe investments such as government bonds. Economic turbulence could prompt the Federal Reserve to hold off on raising interest rates until next year, which would likely help keep mortgage rates low, economists said.

Economists said major global economic shocks tend to boost refinancing activity because homeowners have learned over the years to expect the possibility that rates will fall. Some economists and executives said they expect the impact will be longer lasting than that of other recent shocks, such as the Greek debt crisis several years ago or the Chinese stock-market tumble last August and this January.

"I think this will have some legs on it," said Mr. Walters of Quicken Loans.

Stress Test Inc.: The Brisk Business of Bank Consulting

By Ryan Tracy 
1,061 words
29 June 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- After Citigroup Inc. unexpectedly failed the Federal Reserve's annual stress tests in March 2014, the bank opened its checkbook and called in the consultants.

The firm had to address several of the regulator's concerns in a short time frame, and no single consulting firm could do the job, according to people familiar with the matter. The bank hired multiple firms and said it spent about $180 million on stress tests during the second half of 2014. The next year, it passed.

The stress tests -- designed to determine whether banks can withstand severe economic shocks -- have made U.S. banks stronger. Also more robust is the multibillion-dollar industry that has developed around the annual exercise. Last week, the Fed said the 33 largest U.S. banks cleared the first round of stress tests. On Wednesday, the firms will find out which ones can boost dividends or buy back shares.

Globally, banks spent about $29 billion on consultants last year, much of that for stress tests in the U.S. and elsewhere, according to analysis firm ALM Intelligence. That compares with $16.35 billion in 2007. The total has increased every year since 2009, a period when banks have been relentlessly cutting expenses and employees in areas such as trading and branches.

The Big Four accounting firms and Big Three consulting firms all have built practices offering manpower and advice to pass regulatory muster. Big financial advisers are also in on the act. Last year, BlackRock Inc. sold forecasting help to about two-thirds of the banks taking the tests.

There are even consultants offering advice on the most economical use of other stress-test consultants.

"Kick out the consultants," risk analysis firm Novantas advertised recently, telling banks it can help them cut back.

The industry has sprung up because failing the stress tests, which the Fed made mandatory in their current form in 2011, can have grave consequences. Banks that don't pass the test can be limited in their ability to raise dividends or buy back shares.

The early versions of the tests helped stem the global financial crisis in 2009 by showing big banks weren't about to collapse. Since then, both regulators and bankers said the exams have made banks such as Citigroup better able to weather shocks like the British vote to leave the European Union.

One executive said this year's scenario -- which included negative interest rates, along with a 10% unemployment rate and a roughly 50% drop in the stock market -- helped catch flaws in systems that weren't ready to compute rates below zero.

But many question whether the Fed is going overboard. Rob Nichols, president of industry group the American Bankers Association, last week called the tests "unrealistically severe" and "unnecessarily opaque." The Fed has made the tests harder to pass each year and surprised banks such as Citigroup by flunking them for risk-management practices. Hence, the need for heavy spending each round to adapt to the new versions of the tests.

Banks are expected to quantify precisely how much money they would lose under the scenarios, a herculean task for firms with global footprints, taking hundreds of mathematical models to predict the behavior of millions of assets. Each loan or investment has unique characteristics a bank must gather, track, check and recheck.

Banks generally underinvested in those skills before 2008, one reason they were hit so hard by the downturn.

The test submissions include a "narrative" document the size of a thick paperback, describing what the firm has done, accompanied by thousands of pages of documentation. Some banks employ professional writers to pen the narratives' chapters, so the Fed will have fewer questions when it examines the firms.

In all, building a stress-test program can cost banks $150 million to $250 million apiece at the start.

Financial firms globally will spend $4 billion on stress-test information technology this year, estimates Chartist Research. That is up from $3 billion in 2014 and is expected to increase 15% in 2017.

Forecasting models generally range in cost from about $100,000 to more than $1 million, depending on their complexity.

"We offer customized and off-the-shelf models to help you calculate the stressed probability of defaults," reads a "Stress Testing Suites" brochure from Moody's Analytics Inc., a unit of Moody's Corp.

The banks taking the Fed exams treat stress-test preparation like its own business line, often paying the executives who lead the effort seven-figure salaries.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the largest U.S. bank by assets, last year had about 550 people working solely on the Fed stress tests, with more than 2,000 employees contributing indirectly, according to a person familiar with the matter.

"It's like a whole new department at the bank," said Joe Sullivan, president of International Market Recruiters, which helps banks fill stress-testing jobs.

Stress has become a problem among stress-test employees, who often race to finish the exams before the Fed's deadline. The night before submissions are due, bank employees have had to stay up loading documents onto the Fed's slow-moving website, one of many late nights.

The test due date for years was January, notoriously ruining bankers' year-end holidays. In sympathy, the Fed this year changed the tests' due date to April, though one banker pointed out that conflicted with spring breaks instead.

Ultimately, the goal is for banks to scale back this mammoth exercise, for executives to have a kind of "push-button" ability to quickly assess their risk, said Michael Alix, who was a senior executive at Bear Stearns during its near-collapse in 2008 and a former Federal Reserve Bank of New York official who now advises banks on the tests as financial services consulting risk leader at PricewaterhouseCoopers.

Banks continue to build up their teams.

The posting for a Citigroup job in Mumbai analyzing the bank's risks there and reporting up to the U.S. parent asks for: "Strong attention to detail, willingness to 'roll up sleeves' and produce a polished, high quality, accurate product; tireless work ethic with ability to work well under pressure."

---

Emily Glazer and Sarah Krouse contributed to this article.

General Atlantic LLC invested in insurance software company Insurity Inc., joining existing investors TA Associates Management LP and Genstar Capital LLC.

Hartford, Conn.-based Insurity makes software for insurance policy administration, claims, billing and analytics. The company also provides related services such as information-technology infrastructure management and compliance reporting for businesses.

General Atlantic Principal Paul Stamas will join Insurity's board of directors, according to a news release.

San Francisco firm Genstar acquired Insurity, previously the insurance software business of LexisNexis Group, in 2011. Genstar sold a stake in the company to Boston-based TA Associates in 2014.

Insurity this month "http://insurity.com/tropics/", a software maker focused on workers' compensation.

Founded in 1980, General Atlantic provides growth equity to businesses, with investments in the business services, financial services, health-care, technology, retail and consumer sectors.

TA Associates invests $50 million to $50 million of equity and $10 million to $50 million for subordinated debt transactions in the business-services, financial-services, consumer and technology sectors. The transactions typically value companies at $100 million to $3 billion.

Genstar invests $50 million to $300 million in companies with enterprise value of $50 million to $1 billion and earnings before interests, taxes, depreciation and amortization of more than $10 million. The firm targets companies in the U.S. and Canada within the health-care, software, financial-services and industrial-technology sectors.

Pro Private Markets
Pamlico Gets FTC Approval to Back Digital Rewards Company

By Laura Kreutzer 
354 words
29 June 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Pamlico Capital Management, which traces it roots to the private-equity arm of Wachovia Corp., received regulatory clearance to back PrizeLogic LLC, according to an early antitrust filing with the Federal Trade Commission.

The FTC issues early antitrust notifications when the agency and the U.S. Department of Justice don't think a proposed deal, if completed, would violate U.S. antitrust laws. Early antitrust clearance doesn't necessarily indicate a deal has closed or will close.

Pamlico secured clearance to purchase the stake in PrizeLogic from shareholder Kenneth Barnett, who is the global chief executive of Mars Agency and an advisory board member of early-stage venture firm Mars Ventures. Mr. Barnett also has owned or continues to own other digital marketing companies, including social marketing company Collective Bias Inc. and Triad Retail Media, which was sold to an investment banking team in 2009, according to his biography on Mars Ventures' website.

According to the filing, Pamlico would invest in PrizeLogic from Pamlico Capital III LP, a $650 million fund the firm raised in 2013. The Charlotte, N.C., firm typically invests $20 million to $100 million of equity per deal in companies with annual revenue of $15 million to $200 million.

Founded in 2008, PrizeLogic offers digital-rewards programs and promotion-management services to a range of corporate clients, including PepsiCo., MillerCoors, Subway Group and Walt Disney Co. In addition to its corporate locations in Southfield, Mich., and Scottsdale, Ariz., the company has offices in New York, Chicago, Los Angeles and Atlanta.

In the past year, the company has been beefing up its senior management team. Last year, it hired Jeff Blackman as its first chief financial officer. Mr. Blackman previously spent seven years as CFO at Foresee Results Inc., a customer experience analytics company that was sold to Answers Corp. in 2013. Foresee was backed by Updata Partners and Investor Growth Capital before its sale to Answers.

Pamlico, which began investing in private equity in 1988, spun out of its former corporate parent, Wachovia, in 2010.

Big-data software company Talend SA disclosed plans for an initial public offering of American Depositary Shares.

The maker of the Talend Data Fabric platform expects to benefit as heavy use of social media and the adoption of internet-of-things technology help produce "massive quantities of new data."

In a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/garlands-take-few-easy-exits-this-year-1467027005", no U.S. tech firms went public in the first quarter. One factor is the performance of 2015 IPOs, as some startups fell short of their private valuations. But Twilio Inc.'s recent strong debut was a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/twilio-shares-surge-in-trading-debut-1466693449".

"http://wsj.com/pro/venturecapital/graphics/ipo-index", which measures the stock performance of VC-backed companies that have gone public, has been struggling to recover from a significant drop since February.

Talend, based in Suresnes, France, has its main U.S. operations in Redwood City, Calif.

Talend said its technology "separates the design of integration jobs from the actual code, which enables our platform to work with new technologies as they emerge, such as Hadoop and Spark."

The company's largest shareholders include Silver Lake Sumeru, Balderton Capital and Idinvest Partners. In 2013, when Talend closed a $40 million funding round, Chief Executive Mike Tuchen said it was "building towards" a potential IPO.

Last year, Talend's revenue was $76 million, up from $62.6 million in 2014. Net loss was $22 million, compared with a $22.5 million loss in 2014.

Talend filed confidential IPO paperwork in March. On Tuesday, it filed to offer up to $86.3 million of ADSs, but that is a placeholder amount likely to change. It plans to list its ADSs on Nasdaq under the symbol TLND.

Goldman Sachs, JP Morgan, Barclays and Citigroup are joint book-running managers.

COMPANIES
Anki wins $52m ahead of toy AI robot launch; Technology

By Tim Bradshaw in San Francisco 
360 words
28 June 2016
Financial Times
FTFTA
USA Ed1
19
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Anki, a San Francisco start-up whose toys are powered by artificial intelligence, has raised $52.5m in its latest funding round, as it unveils its latest robot, Cozmo.

JPMorgan led the round, alongside Silicon Valley venture capital firms Andreessen Horowitz and Index Ventures, bringing the six-year-old company's total investment to $182.5m.

Even as tech funding has waned in some once-hot areas such as ondemand delivery apps, venture capital has flooded into AI start-ups over the past 18 months.

The ability to process large amounts of data more quickly and cheaply, thanks to ever-increasing processing power used in smartphones and high-resolution graphics in video games, has spawned hundreds of AI start-ups focused on areas ranging from virtual assistants to self-driving cars.

Anki's staff includes AI experts from research labs such as Carnegie Mellon University's Robotics Institute as well as former animators from Walt Disney's Pixar animation studio. With its first product, Drive, Anki created "smart" robot car racing, combining elements of video games into a physical set of cars and track, all controlled by a mobile device. Cozmo, which will go on sale later this year, takes inspiration from Hollywood robots such as Star Wars ' R2-D2 and Pixar animated film Wall-E .

The diminutive robot, little bigger than a Matchbox car, is able to recognise faces, compete in tabletop games against human players and learn new tricks via a connected smartphone. It also has an "emotion engine" which Anki says will give it an unpredictable personality, depending on how customers play with it.

"When you look at robots in movies - whether that's R2-D2 or Johnny 5 from Short Circuit or Wall-E - they all have a very well-defined personality," says Hanns Tappeiner , Anki's co-founder and president. "The idea for Cozmo is to create a real-life robotic character the way you would normally only see in movies."

Anki will start taking orders for the $179 toy this week before it is released in October.

Stress Test Inc.: Billions of Dollars, Bank Consultants to Manage Other Consultants; Multibillion-dollar industry has developed around the annual exercise created to make U.S. banks stronger

Some banks employ professional writers to pen the narratives' chapters, so the Fed will have fewer questions when it examines the firms.

In all, building a stress-test program can cost banks $150 million to $250 million apiece at the start.

Financial firms globally will spend $4 billion on stress-test information technology this year, estimates Chartist Research. That is up from $3 billion in 2014 and is expected to increase 15% in 2017.

Forecasting models generally range in cost from about $100,000 to more than $1 million, depending on their complexity.

"We offer customized and off-the-shelf models to help you calculate the stressed probability of defaults," reads a "Stress Testing Suites" brochure from Moody's Analytics Inc., a unit of Moody's Corp.

The banks taking the Fed exams treat stress-test preparation like its own business line, often paying the executives who lead the effort seven-figure salaries.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the largest U.S. bank by assets, last year had about 550 people working solely on the Fed stress tests, with more than 2,000 employees contributing indirectly, according to a person familiar with the matter.

"It's like a whole new department at the bank," said Joe Sullivan, president of International Market Recruiters, which helps banks fill stress-testing jobs.

Stress has become a problem among stress-test employees, who often race to finish the exams before the Fed's deadline. The night before submissions are due, bank employees have had to stay up loading documents onto the Fed's slow-moving website, one of many late nights.

The test due date for years was January, notoriously ruining bankers' year-end holidays. In sympathy, the Fed this year changed the tests' due date to April, though one banker pointed out that conflicted with spring breaks instead.

Ultimately, the goal is for banks to scale back this mammoth exercise, for executives to have a kind of "push-button" ability to quickly assess their risk, said Michael Alix, who was a senior executive at Bear Stearns during its near-collapse in 2008 and a former Federal Reserve Bank of New York official who now advises banks on the tests as financial services consulting risk leader at PricewaterhouseCoopers.

After the tests are submitted, the Fed comes to question banks about them. Consultants aren't allowed in the room.

Banks continue to build up their teams.

The "http://jobs.citi.com/in/mumbai/risk-management/jobid10227050-capital-comprehensive-analysis-review-(ccar)-risk-weighted-assets-(rwa)-forecasting-assistant-vice-president-jobs" in Mumbai analyzing the bank's risks there and reporting up to the U.S. parent asks for: "Strong attention to detail, willingness to  roll up sleeves' and produce a polished, high quality, accurate product; tireless work ethic with ability to work well under pressure."

Emily Glazer and Sarah Krouse contributed to this article.

Anki Scoops Up $52.5M for Robot With Personality; The palm-size toy robot, due in October, will recognize faces, play games and express a range of emotions

By Nathan Olivarez-Giles 
644 words
28 June 2016
17:23
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

If you grew up watching movies like "Star Wars," "Short Circuit" or "WALL-E," you've probably daydreamed of what it'd be like to have a robot sidekick, a machine that genuinely reacts to you, recognizes you and talks back.

The team at Anki, a San Francisco startup known for "http://blogs.wsj.com/personal-technology/2015/09/25/anki-overdrive-review-the-future-of-fun/", had the same dream. Their new product isn't a car, it is a friendly robot named Cozmo.

Anki announced Monday the addition of $52.5 million in Series D funding to bring the robot to consumers. J.P. Morgan led the round, with participation from Andreessen Horowitz, Index Ventures and Two Sigma Ventures. The company has now raised a total of $182.5 million.

"The reason we love the robots we see in movies so much is because they don't just respond with the same canned responses, they surprise you," says Hanns Tappeiner, Anki's president and co-founder. "It's taken years but our technology is finally to a point where we can build a robot with personality and bring some of the things you see in the movies to real life."

Cozmo is cute and palm-sized, and looks a bit like a dump truck with an old tube TV for a head. It moves around on tank-style tracks, and has built-in speakers which play a soundtrack of music that changes based on Cozmo's "emotions" at the time. Anki designed its endearingly goofy moves using the "http://www.autodesk.com/products/maya/overview-dts?s_tnt=69290:1:0", only now the action plays out in real life.

Cozmo's smarts and gameplay are reminiscent of "http://blogs.wsj.com/personal-technology/2015/09/25/anki-overdrive-review-the-future-of-fun/", which let users unlock new characters and attributes as they race. Like Overdrive cars, Cozmo has onboard sensors, but its brains are in the smartphone or tablet used for gameplay. The software in the app includes Cozmo's "emotion engine," some artificial intelligence to handle decision making and computer vision that lets Cozmo know where it is in relation to other objects.

Those emotions are key to Cozmo's appeal. When it recognizes a new person, Cozmo is social and curious. When ignored, it gets sad or plays on its own. It is frustrated when it loses games, and celebrates when it wins.

"Cozmo never gives you the same reaction twice," Mr. Tappeiner said. "The head movement, the arm movement, the ways his eyes animate on his face, or the inflection of his voice the combinations change to create personality."

Mr. Tappeiner says Anki will add more games and even more emotional reaction by the time Cozmo goes on sale for $180 in October. (You can preorder it on anki.com starting Monday.) Down the road, Anki intends to develop story-based games and even give Cozmo a few friends.

Although comparison to WALL-E is obvious, it is hard not to compare Cozmo to another recent favorite, BB-8, star of "Star Wars: The Force Awakens." Sphero "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bb-8-awakens-is-spheros-new-droid-the-most-awesome-star-wars-toy-ever-1441285524", but while its motions and quirky mannerisms were spot on with the film character, Sphero's toy doesn't have sensors, and can't do much to interact with its surroundings, aside from "http://blogs.wsj.com/digits/2016/04/05/the-droid-awakens-spheros-bb-8-toy-reacts-while-watching-latest-star-wars-film".

Cozmo needs more content so the novelty doesn't wear off. And while Cozmo can't say much more than my name, other toys are starting to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/talking-toys-are-getting-smarter-should-we-be-worried-1450378215". But already Anki is taking toy robots to a new level, where they act, and even think, much more like their Hollywood counterparts.

Schlesinger's Take: Brexit and Financial-Stability Risks

Treasury's Jacob Lew: Brexit Not Developing into a Financial Crisis

Banks Get Reprieve From Brexit Hammering

SEC Drafting Rule Requiring Firms Reveal Board Diversity

Hillary Clinton Lauds CFPB in Appearance With Elizabeth Warren

Brexit and Financial-Stability Risks

It's hard to feel too relaxed with "http://www.wsj.com/articles/european-markets-remain-pressured-by-brexit-1467013530" losing a record $3 trillion over the past two trading days. But "http://www.wsj.com/articles/treasurys-jacob-lew-brexit-not-developing-into-a-financial-crisis-1467034882" on Monday offered what passes for consensus reassurance in the wake of the Brexit bombshell, telling CNBC there's "no sense of a financial crisis developing" and "it's been an orderly impact so far."

"So far" is the operative phrase. There has been much exhaling since Friday about how the U.K. vote to leave the European Union hasn't become a "Lehman moment." And that is true in the literal sense that markets have functioned much more smoothly since June 24 than after Sept. 15, 2008.

But what if this is a Bear Stearns moment? (The March 2008 collapse seemed to pass without broader repercussions but actually presaged the bigger meltdown of Lehman Brothers later in the year.) Or the Thai baht moment? (The July 1997 devaluation triggered the Asia crisis-turned-emerging market crisis.) Or the Long-Term Capital Management moment? (The 1998 hedge-fund blow-up stoked fears of a destructive chain reaction across Wall Street).

Maybe the postcrisis regulatory regime has inoculated the system against such contagions, as the optimists argue. Or maybe it's just too soon to know. Here are some signs of strains, echoing earlier crises, to monitor in coming days.

Hedge Funds: No major funds have yet reported big losses, and many boasted after the fact that they had learned their lessons from other recent shocks "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hedge-funds-limited-risk-ahead-of-brexit-vote-1467045278". Perhaps. Or it could just be too soon to know how badly they were hit. Goldman Sachs issued a note Friday "http://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2016/06/27/hedge-funds-bought-european-shares-in-run-up-to-brexit/" by the firm's hedge-fund clients ahead of the referendum. Bank of America reported its hedge-fund clients were holding bullish stakes in emerging markets, which have been hit particularly hard post-Brexit, warning of a "long squeeze."

European Banks: "Just a few years ago, Europe's banks managed to stagger out of crisis brought on by the Continent's debt woes," "http://www.wsj.com/articles/new-risks-for-european-lenders-1466973158". Brexit "could push them back in" due to factors from lingering bad debts to renewed fears of a eurozone breakup. Italy's government is worried enough to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/rome-mulls-capital-injection-to-support-italian-banks-after-brexit-shock-1467021718" into its banking system.

Emerging Markets: The "http://www.wsj.com/articles/brexit-effect-ripples-through-emerging-markets-1467033481" from South Africa to Poland to Mexico, as investors fretted, among other things, that the stronger dollar would increase debt burdens as dollar-denominated debt becomes more expensive to service and pay back. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/brexit-upends-chinas-plans-for-turmoil-free-summer-1466950552" if the gap between the dollar and the yuan expands, raising risks for a destabilizing acceleration of capital outflows. The last big global market shocks, pre-Brexit, came from similar sharp gyrations in China.

Policy Impotence: Policy makers have been able to cushion the blow from recent shocks including the Lehman moment with aggressive monetary stimulus and big bailouts. But the political and economic hangover from those rescues will make it harder to respond if the next crisis hits soon. One particularly worrisome constraint: The negative interest rates central banks have been forced to adopt actually weaken the banking sector, by pinching margins for lenders.

As Claudio Borio, chief economist at the Bank for International Settlements, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bank-for-international-settlements-warns-of-risks-to-global-growth-1466937001": "The room for maneuver has narrowed."
