Pimco to lay off 3% of staff

Stephen Foley 
690 words
16 June 2016
18:36
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Pimco, the bond fund manager, is laying off 3 per cent of its global staff as it continues to shrink in the wake of the departure of founder Bill Gross.Affected employees   the majority in business rather than investment roles   were told earlier today, reports Stephen Foley in New York.

The company said it employed 2,300 people at the end of March, after trimming the workforce slowly since Mr Gross s departure in September 2014 by not replacing staff who leave.

The company said that it had moved on to compulsory layoffs, in a statement issued today.

The FT has obtained the internal memo sent to staff by Dan Ivascyn, chief investment officer, Doug Hodges, chief executive, and Jay Jacobs, president. Read in full below:

Colleagues,

Earlier today the Executive Committee directed a series of steps to ensure that our business is better structured to serve our clients in this fast-changing asset management industry landscape. We want to share this news with you now that we have connected with our colleagues who are affected.

As a primary step, we have taken the difficult decision to reduce our headcount by 68 people. We have always strived to hire top talent, so those who are now leaving are colleagues who have given tremendously to PIMCO and our clients. We pride ourselves on being a lean organization with a special culture, so that only makes this decision even more difficult and personal for us as a firm. While this reduction represents just 3 percent of our workforce, the changes are especially painful given the close bonds that all of us forge with one another as colleagues and friends. We thank all those who are leaving for their many contributions and we will miss them.

Secondly, we will be offering voluntary severance to eligible employees in the US. Those eligible will be contacted later today by HR. Separation terms will be determined by reference to local custom and regulations.

Finally, we have decided to restructure our active Dividend strategies by converting the equity exposure to Research Affiliates Equity Income (RAE Income). While our Dividend team colleagues have made important contributions to the firm, we believe this conversion is a better outcome for clients as we seek consistent performance with a lower risk profile, while delivering on the objectives of high current yield and long-term capital appreciation. As a result, the dividend team will be among our colleagues referenced above who are leaving the firm.

Here is why we are taking these measures: We remain committed to first caring for the assets that clients have entrusted to us. We also see growth potential in a range of areas including alternatives, private credit, solutions, client analytics, and regionally in many parts of the world, not to mention the increasing investor demand for many of our non-traditional strategies. We know that the role of active management has never been more important to client portfolios. The competitive demands of this industry require that we continually adapt and innovate to meet evolving client needs. Therefore, everything we do is focused on providing investment performance, innovative solutions for clients and outstanding client service.

Of note, as we collectively move forward as a firm, we will continue to build out teams and hire the best talent globally and manage our staff levels through attrition, hiring, repositioning of resources and through promotion and compensation. We have undertaken an extensive benchmarking exercise this year to compare our compensation with competitors and will use this to remain a top payer in our industry.

Taking these actions today positions all of us for the future. We will use our substantial resources to invest in you and add to our already remarkable talent where necessary.

Today is a difficult day for all of us at PIMCO, but most importantly, for those affected by these decisions. The firm is providing support to those affected and we thank them again for their contributions.

We thank you all for your continued commitment to our clients and the firm as we move forward.

Dan, Doug and Jay

Pimco lays off 3% of global workforce after client outflows

Stephen Foley in New York 
459 words
16 June 2016
20:08
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Pimco has laid off 3 per cent of its global workforce and has asked for more staff to volunteer to leave, as it continues to deal with the fallout of founder "http://www.ft.com/intl/topics/people/Bill_Gross" s damaging exit in 2014.

The fixed income-focused fund management firm s assets under management have shrunk by about $600bn to $1.5tn, the result of client outflows.

Pimco, which is owned by Allianz of Germany, employed some 2,450 people at its peak and has been trimming the workforce slowly since Mr Gross s departure in September 2014 by not replacing staff who leave.

Its executive committee decided to move on to compulsory lay-offs and informed 68 staff on Thursday that they were no longer needed.

The axe falls most heavily on the business side of the firm, although investment manager Brad Kinkelaar who runs dividend-paying equities portfolios will also be leaving.

 The competitive demands of this industry require that we continually adapt and innovate to meet evolving client needs,  staff were told in an "http://on.ft.com/1Yv03Cu" signed by Dan Ivascyn, chief investment officer, Doug Hodge, chief executive, and Jay Jacobs, president.

The memo went on to say that Pimco would still hire in areas where it sees growth potential, although it has also contacted several dozen additional staff in the US to encourage them to apply for voluntary severance.

Outflows have come largely from the core bond portfolios formerly run by Mr Gross. While Pimco has been working to familiarise clients with a younger generation of star managers, it has also been struggling against concerns over the future direction of fixed income markets, where rising interest rates could hurt core bond portfolios in particular.

The company has lost market share to rivals such as MetWest and DoubleLine, while a Vanguard bond market tracker fund last year stole Pimco s title as the world largest fixed income fund.

The internal memo went on:  We see growth potential in a range of areas including alternatives, private credit, solutions, client analytics, and regionally in many parts of the world, not to mention the increasing investor demand for many of our non-traditional strategies. 

The rise of low-cost index funds and a growing disillusionment with high fees have roiled the industry, and there have been cost cuts at many firms, particularly those focused on active fund management.

BlackRock laid off 400 staff earlier this year, and Franklin Resources has set cost-cutting targets in response to big outflows. GMO, the Boston-based fund manager run by Jeremy Grantham, has also cut 10 per cent of its 650-person staff, according to a research report on the firm by Morningstar.

Sarah Krouse

SWITZERLAND

Big Banks Warned On Capital Levels

Switzerland's central bank said on Thursday that the country's two biggest lenders, UBS Group AG and Credit Suisse Group AG, need to bolster their respective layers of protective capital.

The Swiss National Bank said in its annual Financial Stability Report that while both big banks have met requirements designed to protect the Swiss economy in the case of a large bank failure, UBS and Credit Suisse each have work to do in order to meet recently revised rules that go into effect in 2020.

To meet requirements for leverage ratios, or capital held relative to assets and investments, UBS and Credit Suisse each need additional going-concern capital--which can absorb losses under normal operating conditions--of roughly 10 billion Swiss francs ($10.3 billion), the SNB said. It added that the banks could cover most of this requirement by issuing so-called contingent convertible debt, designed to turn into equity if a bank's capital levels fall beneath a designated level.

UBS said in a statement that while it disagrees  with a number of depictions" in the report, it  does not point out anything materially new." Credit Suisse didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

John Letzing

The image-discovery site said Fleksy's technology isn't part of the deal, and the app will continue to run for now. Instead, Pinterest said about half of Fleksy's 10-person staff will join the company, including founder and CEO Kosta Eleftheriou.

Terms of the deal weren't disclosed.

San Francisco-based Fleksy raised about $6 million from investors including Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and Highland Capital, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

Founded in 2011, Fleksy started off as an iPhone and iPad app for the blind community to help them type on devices. It later expanded to serve a broader audience for both Apple iOS and Google Android devices, helping users to improve the speed of typing and enabling them to customize their keyboard.

Pinterest said some of the app's accessibility components that aid the blind and visually impaired with be open sourced. Fleksy has been downloaded on more than 12 million devices, Pinterest said.

The predictive technology powering smart keyboard space has been getting crowded with options such as SwiftKey and Swype vying to become the consumer's first choice.

Competition heated up when Microsoft Corp. "http://blogs.wsj.com/digits/2016/02/03/microsoft-buys-swiftkey-the-latest-deal-in-ai-hungry-silicon-valley/" for a reported $250 million in February, as ambitions in artificial intelligence have grown among the tech giants.

 Pinterest is intensely focused on refining the mobile experiences that make it possible to flow effortlessly through our catalog of ideas on any device, any place in the world," said Scott Goodson, Pinterest's head of core experience.  The Fleksy team is made up of truly noteworthy engineers and world experts in their areas. These technical artists have a history of developing architecturally innovative, highly optimized software."

Pinterest has made several acquisitions in recent years to acquire talent. Last month, Pinterest acquired half of the staff at URX, a San Francisco mobile advertising service.

pict086.png

Life
A New Status Quo for the Boardroom; Ed Garden, chief investment officer of Trian Fund Management, believes the successful management teams of the future will rule through cooperation, not coercion

By Ed Garden 
414 words
15 June 2016
15:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For the better part of a century, there's been a clear separation between the ownership and the management of publicly traded companies. Most shareholders haven't individually owned enough stock or had the expertise to credibly demand a say in decision-making. As a result, most owners have been passive; if they disagree with the direction of a company, their only option has been to sell.

"http://graphics.wsj.com/image-grid/future-of-everything/"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/are-slot-machines-about-to-get-smart-1465997822"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/whats-next-for-artificial-intelligence-1465827619?tesla=y"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-man-who-greenlighted-game-of-thrones-on-televisions-future-1465827910"

* "http://graphics.wsj.com/image-grid/future-of-everything/"

But all that is changing. First, institutional shareholders have gotten bigger, and their ownership of corporate America has become more concentrated. These institutions invest the savings of many Americans, and the amount they manage has swelled. Second, many of these institutional shareholders have developed deep industry expertise, truly understanding the industries in which they invest. Third, poor performance by management and outright cases of abuse of power and even fraud have created a mindset among shareholders that management teams have lost their right to be left alone. Lastly, there are a few shareholders, commonly known as  activists" but whom I call  highly engaged shareowners," who are galvanizing their fellow shareholders and providing the catalyst for management to be held accountable.

Critics have accused so-called activists of advocating business decisions that create a short-term bump in stock prices at the expense of the company's long-term health, and some politicians would like to legislate against the rising influence of shareholders, claiming that it leads to short-termism, rising inequality and low pay for workers.

But in my view, the management and directors of public companies, not the shareholders, have driven such short-term behavior, and the way to build strong companies and create jobs is not through government mandate or protecting weak management teams. It will happen because market forces will reward the companies in which management teams and highly engaged shareowners work together to achieve sustained, lasting growth.

There's no going back: Public shareowners are becoming more involved, more informed and more engaged. That will lead to better long-term growth and performance, a win-win for all constituencies, including customers, employees, shareholders and management.

FirstMark Capital, a New York City firm that has invested in companies such as Pinterest and Shopify, has raised $480 million for two new venture-capital funds.

The growth fund will invest at Series B, C or D in startups both from its own early-stage funds as well as companies outside its funds.  It's for anything we believe has established itself as a winner," Mr. Heitzmann said.  These companies have clearly broken out and established themselves as iconic companies."

Some companies from the firm's first opportunity fund include Airbnb; Pinterest; InVision; and Tapad, which agreed to be "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120160201ec21a2ip8" for $360 million in February.

Mr. Heitzmann said that despite launching the growth fund, the firm has been careful amid the rush to invest in high valuation late-stage companies, also known as unicorns. A number of the growth fund companies are from the firm's early-stage fund that they know well, he said.

 We've been cautious," he said.  We've been very selective on companies that are winners."

FirstMark, one of the larger venture firms in New York City, is bullish on local startups, having invested about 50% of its deals there, while also investing 25% of its deals in the San Francisco Bay Area.

 When we started in 2008, people said they aren't sure New York is big enough a market to start a VC firm," Mr. Heitzmann said.  That's changed over the past eight years and it's gotten to the point where its sustainable and building on itself."

New York City was the second largest U.S. startup market by deals with 137 to the San Francisco Bay Area's 264 in the first quarter of 2016 and by dollars, with $2.2 billion to the Bay Area's $3.9 billion, according to industry tracker Dow Jones VentureSource.

FirstMark is probably best known for its early-stage investment in Pinterest. The firm has also invested in Riot Games, which was acquired by Tencent; X.ai; Lumosity; Knewton; Shopify and Hyperscience.

The firm prefers to invest in serial entrepreneurs as well as people the firm has worked with before or known for a number of years. Areas of focus include big data, artificial intelligence, marketplaces, as well as social, enterprise, software as a service, health care and education.

FirstMark has aimed to build a tech community with monthly New York City events on big data, design and other topics, as well as services for its companies in recruiting, customer service and other areas.

The firm's managing directors are Mr. Heitzmann, Amish Jani, and Matt Turck. Two partners in the firm's last fund, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120151130ebbum9ygl" and Gerald Poch, aren't in the new fund. The firm doesn't have immediate plans to add new partners, but could do so if the right candidate is available, Heitzmann said.

Neil Woodford has built up a 21 per cent stake in Imperial Innovations, a technology-focused investment company, underlining the fund manager s commitment to backing small British companies with scientific expertise.

Mr Woodford, the UK s best known fund manager who won over investors during his 25 years at Invesco Perpetual by turning a 1,000 investment into 23,000, has acquired 2,739,604 ordinary shares in Imperial Innovations.

The Aim-listed company, which has links to researchers at Imperial College London, the University of Oxford and the University of Cambridge has stakes in a range of early stage science and technology companies.

These include Yoyo, a smartphone app that acts as a virtual wallet; Cell Medica, which is developing treatment for post bone-marrow transplantation viral infections; and Feature Space, a data analytics company seeking to predict how people react to situations based on behavioural patterns.

According to Woodford Investment Management, the company set up by Mr Woodford in 2014, the stake in Imperial Innovations is divided between his two funds, Woodford Equity Income and the Patient Capital Trust.

The technology investment company accounts for 1.4 per cent of Woodford Equity Income and 2.4 per cent of Patient Capital Trust.

Analysts said the 11.6m holding makes more sense for Patient Capital Trust, which invests in a mix of disruptive early-stage companies, as well as mid- and large-capitalisation companies.

But Jason Hollands, managing director of UK investment adviser Tilney Bestinvest, raised questions about the stake held in the larger Woodford Equity Income fund.

Imperial Innovations made a pre-tax loss of 5.9m in the six months to the end of January, and has never paid a dividend. Woodford Equity Income, which has 8.7bn of assets, primarily invests in companies that deliver sustainable dividend growth.

Mr Hollands said:  Imperial Innovations is entirely suited to the Patient Capital Trust. Eight of [the fund s] top 10 holdings are in the healthcare sectors, including three unquoted companies.

 Woodford Equity Income, in contrast, offers  exposure to companies that can deliver sustainable dividend growth and aiming to deliver attractive, positive long-term returns and a growing income stream . A stock like this doesn t naturally fit with that description. 

Darius McDermott, managing director at Chelsea Financial, a fund supermarket, added that the technologies used by many of the companies backed by Imperial Innovations remained  unproven .

But he added:  Neil is a really big believer in the underlying technologies 	. 	. 	. 	If he really likes an idea, he will own a big chunk of it. 

Woodford Investment Management declined to comment.

However, a spokesperson pointed to a "https://woodfordfunds.com/blog/mission-possible/" by analyst Lucinda Crabtree in which she highlighted a company backed by Imperial Innovations   Mission Therapeutics   as  incredibly exciting .

Ms Crabtree said the company, which is developing technology to treat oncology-related conditions and neurodegenerative illnesses such as Parkinson s disease, stood out.

 We strongly believe innovation such as this will have tremendous commercial potential in the future,  she wrote.

LOMBARD
VE Day for US investment banks should be celebrated by clients too

By Jonathan Guthrie 
388 words
10 June 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

American GIs who came to the UK for D-Day 72 years ago were jokingly said to be "overpaid, oversexed and over here". US investment banks have achieved their own victory in Europe in recent years, occupying four out of the top five spots for fees earned , according to analytics group Coalition.

Whether bankers at Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley and Bank of America Merrill Lynch are oversexed is for other pundits to judge. But it is questionable whether the banks themselves are overpaid. European clients shell out fees that are 18 per cent lower than those in the US.

European investment bankers, lacking a credible international champion of their own, tend to regard the disparity as unfair. They argue it is easy for Americans to muscle into their market, brought up to fighting weight by domestic fees as high as 7 per cent for a flotation. But charges may be high because US customers are insensitive to price, rather than because there is a cartel. The greater wonder is that European companies are so value-conscious over deals that may matter critically. One theory is that loss-leading privatisation mandates set Europe's fees artificially low in the '80s.

High US fees might equally be a cultural quirk. Some Americans wear turtleneck sweaters under their shirts and think Seth Rogen is funny.

Either way, the horrid truth Europeans should recognise is that US investment banks are successful here because they deserve to be. Their advice is no worse than local rivals, their distribution will often be better and they have big economies of scale.

Their commitment to staying in the business wins them clients and talented staff. Brands such as Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley and Merrill Lynch were well known in the City at the time of Big Bang three decades ago. British investment banking names like Barings, Kleinwort Benson and SG Warburg were big back then but are now largely historic footnotes.

US customers and stockholders should regard the implicit subsidy they provide to the European clients of Wall Street banks as a form of the Marshall Aid, which, after D-Day, helped rebuild a ravaged continent. The locals weren't notably grateful for that, either.

jonathan.guthrie@ft.com

Markets
White House Officials Gather With Fintech Leaders and Regulators; Administration holds  FinTech Summit  focused on the industry s potential to advance economic policy

By Rachel Witkowski

The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON White House officials met privately with regulators and leaders in the financial technology industry Friday to discuss how the government can work with innovators.

The half-day event called the  FinTech Summit" largely focused on how government agencies can tap into the innovation, in which new firms are offering small-business owners and consumers faster forms of loans and digital payments. Federal regulators have been studying the industry to determine how it can be regulated while still encouraging innovation.

On Friday, the White House took it a step further by inviting some of those regulators and key players  to discuss how fintech can help advance critical economic policy priorities," Adrienne Harris, special assistant to the president for economic policy, wrote in a blog post following the event.

 Technology isn't just changing the financial services industry, it's changing the way consumers and business owners relate to their finances, and the way institutions function in our financial system," said Ms. Harris, who led the event.  From consumers' financial health to growing and managing small businesses; from cybersecurity to helping developing nations prosper; and from big data to blockchain we spent the day identifying those areas where partnership across industries and between the public and private sectors can help advance our financial well-being and economic prosperity."

The summit, which was closed to the news media, included officials from the Treasury Department, the Small Business Administration, and leaders in both fintech and traditional banking.

Commerce Secretary Penny Pritzker led a panel discussion on the government's role in fintech and how those firms are partnering with each other and banks. The Commerce Department recently kicked off its own  innovation" series in which Ms. Pritzker met with fintech companies to encourage public-private partnerships.

Other speakers included Marla Blow, a former top official at the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau who helped start FS Card Inc., a fintech credit-card company with a focus on subprime borrowers. Ms. Blow spoke on the  changing landscape of financial services," according to an agenda obtained by The Wall Street Journal.

Summit participants also discussed the  big data" report released by the White House in May that addressed how such data can be used to offer credit to underserved consumers but also poses civil rights and privacy risks. Roy Austin, deputy assistant to the president for urban affairs, led that discussion, examining how fintech firms use such data.

The event concluded with a discussion on how fintech firms are a  potent enabler" for developing countries. The conversation was led by Gayle Smith, the administrator of the U.S. Agency for International Development.

 Mobile phone-based payment systems are making vulnerable communities more resilient, allowing governments to operate more transparently," Ms. Harris wrote. She also said they could be the foundation for businesses in remote areas  so that households living beyond the electricity grids in East Africa can access clean energy for the first time and Indian villages can develop sustainable water systems."

The panel concluded that the public and private sectors need  to align around a common vision and work together to lay the foundations for modern, inclusive economies," Ms. Harris said.

pict096.png

Lending Club, securitisations and proper loan due diligence

Kadhim Shubber 
1,384 words
9 June 2016
11:46
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

One of the causes of the global financial crisis   and there were many   was just outright fraud.

Fraud when US subprime mortgages were being written; fraud when those mortgages were being packaged into bonds; and "http://www.economist.com/blogs/freeexchange/2013/02/bubbles-and-fraud" when those bonds were being sold to investors.

For example, banks would hire a company to check if loans they were securitising were dodgy, but wouldn t disclose the results of that review to end investors. The reason for this secrecy, as Felix Salmon discussed in a 2010 "http://blogs.reuters.com/felix-salmon/2010/10/13/the-enormous-mortgage-bond-scandal/", is that the banks could demand a discount from the loan originator whenever they found bad loans, pocketing it as a profit instead of passing it on to investors, who were kept in the dark.

And as the world discovered, investors being kept in the dark   whether through malice, greed or stupidity   did not end well.

Today, banks and any other securitisers in the US have to file a Form ABS-15G, which tells investors if there was an independent review of the underlying loans and, if so, what that review flagged up. The regulation requiring this disclosure came into force in June 2015.

But a securitiser can still choose not to have a third party inspect the loans, therefore avoiding having to disclose anything and saving money too.

This appears to be the typical state of affairs for most deals involving loans originated by online lenders.

Even where the reports are available, they show that digitisation is not quite the cure-all to the problem of bad borrower data that some have hoped.

Here s a list of all the online securitisations there have been up to the end of March this year, courtesy of PeerIQ, a data analytics company that tracks the online lending space. Click for the full thing:

The list has 47 deals worth $8.6bn, 23 of which were issued after the ABS-15G requirement came into force in June 2015, meaning that any third-party due diligence reports would have to be disclosed to the Securities and Exchange Commission and made public.

So if there was third-party due diligence, theoretically anyone should be able to see it. But we were able to find just six reports for those 23 deals   if you find more, do let us know   suggesting that the vast majority were checked in-house by the issuer and therefore any problems found in the creation of the deal remain secret. (That count doesn t include deals issued since March.)

The deals that did have third-party due diligence tended to be those issued by the online lender themselves, rather than by an investor who had bought their loans.

For example, SoFi, which has always arranged its own deals, consistently has third-party due diligence. A spokesperson for Avant, whose securitisations this year were independently checked, said the oversight helps reassure investors given its relatively short track record as a securitiser. Similarly, a spokesperson for OnDeck, an online lender to small business, said both of the securitisations it has carried out directly were checked by independent auditors.

By contrast, none of Citi s securitisations of Prosper Marketplace loans appear to have had a third party checking over the loan data. A Fitch Ratings report for the deal priced in March   the last before the relationship rather publicly fell apart   notes that Citi did  all the due diligence in-house . Both Prosper and Citi declined to comment. (As did almost all of the online lenders we contacted.)

But where third-party checks did occur, they were only skin-deep. As PwC states in "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1555110/000089853116000625/slc-abs15g.htm" for a securitisation by SoFi, the checks do not involve any assurances on  the completeness, accuracy, appropriateness, quality or integrity of any of the information  in the loan documents.

All the checks consist of is comparing a spreadsheet full of loan information with the underlying documentation, both of which are ultimately provided by the company that made the loan in the first place. There are no efforts to independently check if the information is accurate.

That said, even this rather shallow task throws up interesting discoveries that should make you question the data systems of even the newest fintech companies.

For example, this is from "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1555110/000089706916000889/cg753a.htm" on another SoFi deal, showing the discrepancies in gross income that appeared between the spreadsheet, the  data file value , and the income calculated from the underlying documentation, or  source file value :

For context, that s three from a sample of 600 loans.

There are a few more noted in this report on the "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1555110/000089853116000625/slc-abs15g.htm"   from a smaller sample of 100 loans   including a three-year discrepancy in the graduation date of one borrower (the third from the bottom):

Or perhaps more comically, in this "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1658153/000089706916000580/cg641.htm" on a deal by Earnest, another online lender to students, the discrepancies in a sample of 150 loans include the transformation of a $150,000 a year salary from Blackstone Group into a $450,000 a year salary from Goldman Sachs (if only life were so easy):

"https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1658153/000089706916000832/cg731.htm" by Deloitte for another Earnest deal shows similar mixups in another sample of 150 loans (click to enlarge):

The point here is not that these deals were bad, or that these lenders have less reliable data than anyone else. Rather it s a reminder that even finance companies who have very recently built their systems from scratch don t get it right all of the time.

We ve seen the same thing in the UK. In the process of investigating Ratesetter s loan book and querying certain entries with the company, we found at times that the data were incorrect and could not be taken at face value.

Interestingly, there is also a report for the Jefferies securitisation of Lending Club loans, which would have been the first rated deal involving the company s loans but instead resulted in the departure of chief executive and founder Renaud Laplanche.

In case it s not fresh in your memory, the seeds of the crisis were a borrower documentation change requested by Jefferies. Lending Club duly made the change, but an employee tweaked the date on a batch of loans to make it look like they had been originated after the change had been agreed   thereby making it seem like they qualified for the deal.

The "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1650171/000092963816001434/exhibit99-1.htm", published on "https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1650171/000092963816001434/0000929638-16-001434-index.htm", five full days before Lending Club revealed the scandal and its share price cratered, hints at those events. In the report, Deloitte says it received a listing of loans on April 5th, from which it randomly chose a sample. Then, a few weeks later on April 26th, Deloitte received a data file, likely a spreadsheet, which it would compare with the sample.

But on April 27, the following day, Deloitte received another data file, which contained  an updated origination date for each of the 11,830 unsecured consumer loans  in the initial data file:

We were instructed by representatives of the Company [Jefferies] to replace the origination date information set forth on the Initial Data File with the corresponding information set forth on the Origination Date Supplemental Data File

The report goes on to say that Deloitte found about half of the loans it sampled were missing from the data file. Lending Club and Jefferies declined to comment. Deloitte did not return a request for comment.

As it happened, Lending Club came clean and made a pretty good show to investors that it wouldn t tolerate any messing around with its loan data   even if there seems to be have been more going on than meets the eye.

But relying on finance companies to fess up instead of burying misdeeds has historically been a pretty bad bet, which is why these sorts of third-party reviews are important, even for fintech startups.

The legal tweak that led to disaster for Lending Club   FT Alphaville

There are bigger questions for Lending Club to answer   FT Alphaville

Why Lending Club has shunned securitisations   FT Alphaville

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 122 by 40, depth 1
pict097.png

US investment banks conquer Europe

Jonathan Guthrie 
839 words
9 June 2016
19:29
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

American GIs who came to the UK for D-Day 72 years ago were jokingly said to be  overpaid, oversexed and over here . US investment banks have achieved their own victory in Europe in recent years, occupying four out of the top five spots for fees earned, according to analytics group Coalition.

Whether bankers at Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, and Bank of America Merrill Lynch are oversexed is for other pundits to judge. But it is questionable whether the banks themselves are overpaid. European clients shell out fees that are 18 per cent lower than those in the US.

European investment bankers, lacking a credible international champion of their own, tend to regard the disparity as unfair. They argue it is easy for Americans to muscle into their market, brought up to fighting weight by domestic fees as high as 7 per cent for a flotation.

But charges may be high because US customers are insensitive to price, rather than because there is a cartel. The greater wonder is that European companies are so value-conscious over deals that may matter critically. One theory is that a slew of loss-leading privatisation mandates set European fees artificially low in the 1980s.

High US fees might equally be a cultural quirk. Some Americans wear turtleneck sweaters under their shirts and think Seth Rogen is funny.

Either way, the horrid truth Europeans should recognise is that US investment banks are successful here because they deserve to be. Their advice is no worse than local rivals, their distribution will often be better and they have big economies of scale.

Their commitment to staying in the business wins them clients and talented staff. Brands such as Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley and Merrill Lynch were well known in the City at the time of Big Bang three decades ago. British investment banking names like Barings, Kleinwort Benson and SG Warburg were big back then but are now largely historic footnotes.

US customers and stockholders should regard the implicit subsidy they provide to the European clients of Wall Street banks as a form of the Marshall Aid, which, after D-Day, helped rebuild a ravaged continent. The locals weren t notably grateful for that, either.

Kicker conspiracy

The imminent Euro 2016 football tournament will give fans the chance to debate the salient differences between the English and German version of the games. The playbook for awarding TV rights for football in both countries is identical: players extract astronomic pay from their clubs, which pass on the cost to broadcaster Sky.

The long-suffering News Corp affiliate has just agreed to "http://www.ft.com/fastft/2016/06/09/sky-climbs-after-scoring-german-football-contract/"an average of 80 per cent more to show Bundesliga matches for four seasons at a cost of around  ¬3.5bn. It has lost exclusivity over top-flight German football in the process.

The outcome was as familiar as Germany defeating England in a semi-final. Sky last year agreed to pay 70 per cent more for three seasons of Premiership rights. Back then BT, rather than John Malone s Eurosport, was the deep-pocketed interloper who pushed up the bidding.

The gamble chief executive Jeremy Darroch took with Premiership rights appears justified by strong earnings and a declining debt burden. At best he can be credited with buying time for Sky, a medium-sized business whose fortunes are inextricably linked to a big money game. Mr Darroch himself is likely to have moved on before costs become truly unsustainable.

Kiwi combo

Flight of the Conchords, a sitcom about Kiwis adrift among New Yorkers ignorant of their nation, spawned the wry slogan:  New Zealand: it s not part of Australia . Nor is New Zealand s Sky Network Television part of the UK s Sky, mentioned above. But the group is set to become a subsidiary of deal-hungry telecoms group Vodafone through a NZ$3.4bn merger, which, for readability, we ll convert to US$2.4bn.

British companies have been hanging back from M&A ahead of the Brexit vote. Vodafone is going ahead with this deal, though in a place about as far as you can get from Nigel Farage without boarding a spaceship.

Telephone air time is increasingly commodified. There is fierce competition in pay TV. The combination of SNT and Voda s New Zealand operations is expected to yield cost and capital expenditure synergies with a net present value of $295m.

The partners are promoting the deal as a merger, though it can also be seen as a reverse takeover by Vodafone. The UK group will own 51 per cent of a group that will retain SNT s New Zealand listing. SNT, which is smaller than Vodafone New Zealand, is paying the parent $850m in debt-financed cash and the balance of the $2.4bn consideration in its own shares. These will be issued at a premium to the undisturbed market price, implying Voda is contributing its Kiwi cash flows to the combined group at a discount.

COMPANIES
Fundraising values web security start-up at $1bn; Technology

By Hannah Kuchler in San Francisco 
289 words
9 June 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Cylance, a cyber security start-up seeking to upend the old world of antivirus software, has raised $100m from investors including private equity firm Blackstone and Insight Venture Partners.

The fundraising values the company at $1bn, up more than threefold from $300m last year, according to a person close to the deal.

Blackstone invested from its Tactical Opportunities fund, which focuses on complex markets where it believes risk is mispriced.

Stuart McClure , chief executive of Cylance, founded the company after he left Intel Security, the antivirus software maker formerly known as McAfee. He said the group had seen bookings increase more than 1,000 per cent in the past year, and was cash-flow-positive.

Cylance uses artificial intelligence to detect threats to computers and other devices, rather than simply comparing viruses with lists of known malicious software.

Existing investors, including Silicon Valley venture capital firms Khosla Ventures and DFJ Growth, as well as private equity firm KKR, participated in the round.

Cylance's fundraising came after a period when venture capitalists appeared to be holding back from backing cyber security companies. The pause came after VCs spent two to three years pouring cash into start-ups in the sector in the hope that they could solve customers' cyber security problems and earn them millions.

"There's a lot of noise in this space," said Mr McClure. "Deal flow may be starting to come back for now, but it is sort of a tentative and waiting game."

Cylance is not alone in breaking the freeze: Lookout, a start-up focused on protecting smartphones, received an investment from Microsoft for an undisclosed sum recently.

Pro Private Markets
Freenome Raises $5.55M Seed Round For Cancer Detection

430 words
9 June 2016
20:49
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Freenome Inc. has secured $5.55 million to compete with several well-funded companies in a race to develop blood tests that detect cancer in its early stages and guide patients to the best treatments.

Andreessen Horowitz, Data Collective, Founders Fund and Third Kind Venture Capital provided the seed financing.

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/liquid-biopsy-startups-face-long-road-to-payoff-1464003003" that detect DNA and other materials that tumors release into the bloodstream. In September, Piper Jaffray Cos. put the market opportunity for cancer  liquid biopsies" at more than $28 billion.

Most liquid-biopsy companies aim to steer diagnosed cancer patients to the best therapies.

Liquid-biopsy startups aiming for early cancer detection include Grail, which launched in January with $100 million from ARCH Venture Partners, Illumina Inc., and others.

Another player is Guardant Health Inc., which has raised $200 million from Sequoia Capital and others. Guardant, which sells a liquid biopsy to aid treatment selection, is moving into cancer detection through a research program called Lunar.

"http://www.freenome.com/" is developing tests to spot cancer in its early phases, when treatment is most successful. Its tests could also guide therapy decisions. Its process involves analyzing the collection of genetic material floating in the bloodstream, the  freenome." Using a machine-learning system, it identifies genetic characteristics to distinguish between people who have cancer and those who don't.

The technology detects known so-called cancer signatures and uncovers new ones, according to Chief Executive Gabriel Otte. In early research the technology has correctly distinguished between normal and cancerous blood samples. Having validated the approach in hundreds of samples, Freenome now plans testing in thousands of cases, Mr. Otte said. The company plans to publish papers describing how and why its tests work, according to Mr. Otte.

Freenome decided to raise a small amount while vetting its system. It will seek a larger funding depending on how studies progress, Mr. Otte said.

Developing a test is only the first step to success for liquid-biopsy companies. Winning support from regulators and insurers is also vital. Freenome says it is working with Medicare and the Food and Drug Administration on premarket review. The company is also confident insurers will immediately see the benefits of a test that tells doctors which treatment works best.

Company: Freenome

Amount invested: $5.55 million

Series/Round: seed

Investors: Andreessen Horowitz (lead), Founders Fund, Data Collective Venture Capital and Shana Fisher's Third Kind Venture Capital

Company description: Developing a noninvasive blood test to detect cancer.

Company website: "http://www.freenome.com/"

Tech
Tradeshift Raises $75 Million to Connect Global Supply Chains; New funding values company at more than $500 million

By Deborah Gage 
587 words
9 June 2016
06:45
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Thousands of startups are born in Silicon Valley each year, but sometimes the freshest perspectives on new technology come from outsiders.

That is what happened with Tradeshift Holdings Inc., a six-year-old startup that was born in Denmark in 2010, although the company moved its headquarters to San Francisco a year later.

Tradeshift's three founders Christian Lanng, Mikkel Hippe Brun and Gert Sylvest met while working on a Danish government project, a software platform to digitize invoices, which was ultimately adopted by 95% of Denmark's businesses.

That gave them the idea for Tradeshift, which has raised $205 million and is on track this year to generate $50 million in revenue, according to Mr. Lanng, who is the company's chief executive.

Related

* "http://blogs.wsj.com/tech-europe/2010/12/16/tradeshift-aims-to-disrupt-business-banking/" (Dec. 16, 2010)

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00020130125e91pmbsit" (Jan. 25, 2013)

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120150811eb8bnw6fh" (Aug. 11, 2015)

The company's latest funding, a $75 million Series D round, values Tradeshift at more than $500 million.

Tradeshift started after its founders noticed that when companies traded goods or services, especially between countries, their business was often analog and involved sending invoices over email or fax.

 Our core idea was we have Facebook, Twitter and LinkedIn, but when it comes to companies, why don't we connect them in the cloud, in a network that feels more like LinkedIn or Facebook?" Mr. Lanng said.

At first, Tradeshift was just a free social network for businesses, although one that included the ability for members to do business with each other and build apps. The network spread to more than 100 countries.

About 18 months ago, Tradeshift also started selling software that companies could use to connect and manage their supply chains.

Companies can still do business on the network for free, but if they buy Tradeshift's software they get tools to manage complicated issues such as tax compliance and can also create private marketplaces for their suppliers.

Tradeshift now finds itself competing with Oracle Corp. and SAP SE for deals, Mr. Lanng said. More than 800,000 companies use the platform, and Tradeshift has several partnerships, including one with Baiwang, which provides tax-related services in China.

One reason Tradeshift has grown is because of the way the company applies machine learning and large-scale computing to  ingesting, validating, checking for fraud, extracting the signal from and intelligently routing an insane number of invoices," said Data Collective co-founder Matt Ocko, who joins Tradeshift's board.

When the Danish government asked Tradeshift's founders to digitize invoices for the nation's businesses, Mr. Ocko said,  they didn't know that level of automation had never been attempted."

Tradeshift's new funding round includes investments from several banks, because  banks are increasingly not able to access the next generation of business data," Mr. Lanng said.

To be competitive, modern companies need agility, he said, and for that they need software, which is  anachronistic and expensive and they have a hard time doing that."

Tradeshift's new funding was led by Data Collective, which was an early investor in the company, and includes several new investors: HSBC Bank PLC; American Express Ventures; CreditEase Fintech Investment Fund, which is managed by CreditEase; and Pavilion Capital. Current investor Notion Capital also participated in the round.

Write to Deborah Gage at "mailto:Deborah.Gage@dowjones.com"

Technology (A Special Report) --- Does Nasdaq Shine for Chinese Firms?: CEO Bob Greifeld on whether the attraction has faded, and the implications of blockchain

By Gerard Baker 
1,081 words
9 June 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B6
English
Copyright  2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For a long time, for many tech companies, the idea of getting a listing, especially on Nasdaq, was one of their highest goals. In the past few years, though, more companies are choosing to stay private or delay their listings.

Meanwhile, Nasdaq is implementing a big change in the form of blockchain -- a system that underpins the digital currency bitcoin and allows companies to make and verify transactions on a network instantaneously without a central authority.

For a look at what these changes might mean, The Wall Street Journal's Gerard Baker spoke with the chief executive officer of Nasdaq, Bob Greifeld. Here are edited excerpts of their conversation.

MR. BAKER: The phenomenon we've seen in this tech boom is the unicorn -- companies that don't go public, don't list. Or at least they delay their listings. Some of them are already enormous companies. They are weighing out very carefully the pros and cons of a listing, the onerous reporting requirements, against the demand for liquidity. How do you see it?

MR. GREIFELD: We typically counsel companies to think long and hard about going public. When you go public, you're obviously taking in money from shareholders who don't know you. Your reporting requirement as a result of that is quite onerous. And because they don't know you, they have to buy into a fundamental investment thesis. And that investment thesis better match your business model, and that communication has to be clear.

Now that business model does not have to say I'm going to make X% growth quarter after quarter in profits. It could be a model that talks about a story, but then you have to deliver on that story.

As I like to say, as a CEO of a public company, I get my report card every three months, right? We have to report every three months. And if you're not ready for that scrutiny, then you should wait until you are in fact ready to go there. So in terms of the companies that have gone public, that have been successful, the investment thesis matches the operation of the business.

MR. BAKER: Let's talk about China. Nasdaq was a favored destination for a lot of Chinese companies. Companies have pulled out, partly because of some reporting issues and partly because of some scandals there.

What's going on? Are investors more wary of some of these Chinese companies? Are the Chinese companies themselves more nervous?

MR. GREIFELD: We had a rush of Chinese companies that came to market, that did not fully understand the reporting regime and the scrutiny they would be under. So we had a period of delisting of those companies. [Now] our pipeline for Chinese companies coming to the U.S. is as strong as it's been in half a decade. A decade ago, it was really very ebullient in terms of companies rushing to come to the U.S. It's not that, but I think it's at a healthy level.

We think we'll come out with something approaching 10 Chinese companies listing in the U.S. in 2016. That's, I think, a healthy number. And in the context of the fact that the overall U.S. IPO market has been depressed, as a percent it's quite healthy.

MR. BAKER: There had been some issues with a number of very prominent companies, most recently Alibaba, announcing that it was subject to an SEC investigation of some of its accounting practices. Is this raising broader concerns about the reliability of reporting in China?

MR. GREIFELD: I don't know any details on Alibaba, but I think it's also a little bit unfair to say, wow, a Chinese company has a reporting issue, because we would have a very, very long list of American companies that have had reporting issues.

MR. BAKER: But there were a succession of Chinese companies.

MR. GREIFELD: We went through that phase five or six years ago. I think we're at a healthy balance today. We have many companies that have to learn as they go along and get better at reporting.

MR. BAKER: Let's move on to some broader technology questions. Tell us how you are changing. You've talked a lot very excitedly about blockchain and the changes that it's making to the way you can work. Tell us a bit about how that's affecting Nasdaq.

MR. GREIFELD: With respect to Nasdaq, I've always felt that we are a technology company. If you take away the imprimatur, or the exchange license, we're a transaction-processing business. In buzzy terms, we run our business in the cloud. It's software as a service, we get paid per use.

We're on the cusp of a golden age of technology, and it feels exciting. Blockchain and machine learning are really going to change the world that we live in. [With blockchain] you can obviate the need for the trusted intermediaries. And when you start thinking in those terms, it's amazing how many trusted intermediaries there are. That whole middle layer that exists and represents friction in the system will go away.

The first real thing we did with blockchain was in Nasdaq Private Market. In December, we did a trade, a liquidity event, where we traded, settled, cleared and moved the money in 10 minutes. That sounds interesting. It sounds great when you see if you bought or sold Apple that day, it would take three days.

In terms of the limitations of blockchain today, it can handle about 10 transactions per second. In the trading in our market today, we handle millions of transactions a second. So it's apples and oranges. I think where blockchain will come in on the transaction side is when the volume of trading and activity is less active and there's more dimensions to the order.

So, an equity trade is the easiest thing in the world because you've got to buy a thousand shares of Apple at a price. As you get into over-the-counter instruments, derivative instruments, you have a whole series of dimensions to it. And that would be more appropriate for blockchain.
